cycle of nutrition, but with special emphasis on the mating and posklambing 
periods. 
Selected areas may be improved by fencing and grazing control but more 
commonly surface improvements including lime, phosphate and over-sowing 
with grass and clover would be employed. Such improved areas might extend 
to 20—30% of the whole unit and would be grazed to maximise utilisation 
during the critical nutritional periods (Table 6 shows a typical management 
timetable). Such systems thus contain two sub-systems, one of which consists 
of improved grassland while the other consists of unimproved hill land. Sys­
tems of this type are not common in farm practice except at research and 
development centres, but their number is rapidly increasing. It should be noted 
that many hill farms consist of open hill land together with small grass fields 
but the management of these resources differs widely from the intensified 
system described here. 
Nutrient cycling data are given for the same 280-hectare unit described 
under traditional management but which has been operated from 1968 to 
1975 as an intensified system. In 1974/1975 the system consisted of 90 hec­
tares enclosed paddocks (of which 36 ha had received lime and P) together 
part of the area in this system. These values may therefore be in excess of the true losses.

with the original 190 hectares of hill grass-heath. The system now carries 601 
ewes producing 102.7 lambs per 100 ewes. Production is almost double that 
from the traditional system. The system also carries a small number of cattle 
but these are mainly used to clean up uneaten herbage on the production 
paddocks and this does not materially affect the data given in the nutrient 
balance table. This system has recently been described by Eadie et al. (1977).

6.2.3. Systems with forestry 
Classification. Extensive forestry system. Reference: Newbould and Floate-6; 
Meathop Wood, U.K., Table 10. 
The forests managed by the Forestry Commission are predominantly 
coniferous and are located throughout the United Kingdom, with the largest 
proportion (61%) in Scotland. Of the total 1.2 Χ 10
6 ha, 25% is in England 
and the remaining 14% is in Wales. Most of the forests are on the poorer 
soils in the higher and wetter northern and western regions of the country. 
The predominant under-lying soils are strongly podsolized or gleyed, and 
most have a peaty surface horizon. 
In the establishment of such commercial forests (frequently on former 
marginal agricultural land), attention is paid to improving adverse drainage 
conditions and minimising plant competition by the use of special cultivation 
methods. Phosphorus and potassium fertilizer is added either uniformly over 
the whole area to be planted or, more commonly now, to the vicinity of each 
seedling tree. Top dressing of phosphate 6—7 years after planting is often 
practised. It is generally accepted that the critical time nutritionally for 
young trees lasts until the canopies meet at 10—12 years. Provided that the 
trees are in nutrient balance at this time, the system is usually maintained 
without further additions of fertilizer by natural cycling processes. 
There is a much smaller area (200 000 ha) of private mixed woodland, 
and the best data on nutrient cycling available to us refer to a woodland 
ecosystem within this group. We have taken as an example of a woodland 
ecosystem the main U.K. site for study in the IBP Woodland Biome. The 
following description has been given by Satchell (1971): 
"Meathop Woods, covering approximately 40 hectares, lie three kilometres from 
Grange-over-Sands on the sea coast of Morecambe Bay on the southern edge of the 
English Lake District. They are situated at an altitude of about 45 metres on an outcrop 
of Carboniferous limestone with terraces and small scarps. The terraces are covered 
with glacial drift of a mean depth in the study area of 38 cm, but varying greatly with 
the eroded surface of the underlying limestone pavement. The soil is predominantly 
brown earth with a mull humus, the pH of the A horizon in the study area ranging 
from 4.1 to 7.3. 
Meathop Wood is a mixed deciduous wood with oak, ash, birch and sycamore 
dominating the canopy and hazel forming a well-developed understorey. The ground 
layer is dominated by Rubus fruticosus agg., Endymion 
non-scriptus, Anemone 
nemorosa, 
Mercurialis perennis and Oxalis acetosella. There is a recorded history of woodland 
management, mainly for charcoal production, since 1770, and the site is thought to 
have carried woodland indefinitely. Rainfall is approximately 120—125 cm per annum." 
The nutrient balance calculations suggest a gain in soil-N which is probably 
apparent rather than real because there are incomplete data for gaseous ex­
change. The authors state that it is not possible to say whether the system is 
in equilibrium with respect to N. Their scheme suggests that the system as a

ently accumulated in the soil. This latter figure agrees with the present inter­
pretation of the data*. 
Our calculations suggest a net loss of 1.3 kg Ρ ha"
1 per year from the soil, 
which contrasts with an apparent net gain of 0.11 kg Ρ ha"
1 per year in the 
whole system (White and Harrison). The difference between these calculations 
is largely accounted for by incremental gain in the production compartment. 
A fundamental difference between agricultural production systems and 
woodlands is the accumulation of nutrients in the living trees. White and 
Harrison indicate that some 24 kg N, 1.4 kg Ρ and 13 kg Κ ha"
1 y"
1 are incor­
porated into the standing crop and are lost from the soil for the lifetime of 
the tress. In the data in Table 10, these amounts are divided between stems 
(which are eventually lost from the system as product) and root increment 
(which is accounted as a gain in the plant component). 
In both our calculations, and in the White and Harrison presentation, the 
soil appears to be in Κ deficit but this is probably made good by weathering 
of Κ from rocks (no data are available for 16). 
The authors concluded that
4'the only element amongst those sampled (N, 
Ρ, K, Ca, Mg, Na) which is likely to cause stress to the system is phosphorus". 
NOTES TO T A B L E 10. 
(Reference Newbould and Floate-6) 
The data used here have been taken from a draft manuscript of the chapters by Harrison, 
and by White and Harrison in a volume entitled "Studies of an English Oak Woodland" 
(in preparation). 
12. N-fixation estimate from Dr B.T. D'Sylva's laboratory studies. White and Harrison 
(loc. cit.) observe that this may be high but cite data of Jenkinson (1971) who showed 
that when arable soil was allowed to revert to deciduous woodland, 65 kg h a
1 y"
1 Ν 
accumulated in the soil over a period of 81 years. 
15. Data from White and Harrison (loc. cit.). 
17. Mineralization data from White and Harrison for Ν and Ρ : Κ mineralized + Κ humified 
has been used here for mineralization since it is thought unrealistic to consider humification 
of K. 
18. Primary products consist of stems of trees only: stem increment of production from 
White and Harrison (loc. cit.). 
21. Leaching data from White and Harrison (loc.cit.). 
25. White and Harrison give data for humification but in our presentation the amounts in­
cluded here are accounted as litter (26): humification appears in the balance as net gain 
in the soil organic pool. 
26. Divided into (26t) which includes all tree and herb litter, and (26r) which is root death; 
data from White and Harrison (loc. cit.). 
30. Net nutrient uptake interpreted from White and Harrison's data using the following 
assumptions: 
30r = root death + root production increment. 
30t = total uptake by trees and herbs less root uptake and net of loss by stem and leaf 
leaching. 
31. Atmospheric interception by trees does not include leaching from trees or stem wash. 
•Newbould and Floate are greatly indebted to White and Harrison for permission to use 
their data, and to Ulrich and to Harrison who later assisted in interpretation.

6.2.4. Systems with intensive grassland husbandry 
Such systems in the U.K. are mainly concerned with meat production, 
fat lamb or beef, or with dairying. The former are most frequent in areas best 
suited to long-term grassland in the wetter western areas and on traditional 
fattening pastures. The dairying enterprises tend to be concentrated near 
market centres. All these intensive systems are on low ground (<200 m) 
where the climate is moist temperate, and the growing season is usually in 
the range 200—300 days. 
Typical meat-producing enterprises occur in areas where rainfall is in the 
range 800—1200 mm and highest mean monthly temperatures are between 
15 and 18°C. The soils are dominantly brown earth with varying degrees of 
poor drainage, to gleys, and these were formerly associated with deciduous 
woodland. For many centuries these soils have been in agricultural use and 
many have been in permanent or semi-permanent grassland for the past 100 
years. 
In the U.K. there are currently 2.3 Χ 10
6 and 4.9 Χ 10
6 ha of temporary 
and permanent grassland, respectively. Not all of this land is devoted to the 
28.5 Χ 10
6 sheep which produce 240 000 tonnes of mutton and lamb annual­
ly. Some 200 000 ha are classified as mainly sheep farms but a further 
300 000 ha are classified as cattle and sheep enterprises. The average size of 
a farm unit in the U.K. is 45 ha, and the average breeding flock consists of 
162 sheep (Annual Review of Agriculture, 1975). 
To illustrate the principles involved in the operation of grassland sheep 
systems, data have been calculated for a hypothetical intensive lamb produc­
tion system based on either pure grass (plus a large amount of fertilizer N ) or 
mixed grass/clover (plus a small amount of fertilizer N ) swards. 
6.2.4a. Intensive sheep system using mixed pastures of grass and clover with 
little use of nitrogen fertilizer 
Classification. Intensive livestock system. Reference: Newbould and Floate-7; 
U.K. Intensive sheep farming on grass and clover, Table 12. 
Assumptions used to calculate nutrient balances: 
(1) Stocking rate, 7.25 ewes ha"
1. 
(2) Weaning percentage, 160%. 
(3) 120 kg ha"
1 nitrogen fertilizer used each year. 
(4) All lambs sold at 45 kg live weight. 
(5) Bought-in replacement young stock contain equivalent amounts of 
nutrients to the cast ewes and deaths. 
(6) The approximate timetable of the system is shown in Table 11.

6.2.5. Systems with intensive arable farming — winter wheat continuously 
Classification. Intensive arable farming. Reference: Newbould and Floate-9, 
Winter wheat, U.K., Table 14. 
About one-third of the U.K. cereal crop and nearly one-half of the wheat 
crop is grown in eastern England; 3.7 Χ 10
6 ha are devoted to cereal

--- Page 58 ---

55 
production in the U.K. and of this total, 1.2 Χ 10
6 ha give an average yield 
of 7360 kg ha"
1. Although the average farm size in the U.K. is only 45 ha, 
many cereal-producing farms are larger than this overall average. The annual 
average rainfall is less than 650 mm, of which one-half falls in the summer 
months (April—September, inclusive). The yields of wheat in eastern England 
tend, on average, to vary inversely with the total rainfall from June to August. 
The soils in this area are extremely variable, ranging from the Fen peat, to 
silts, breckland, and to heavy stiff clays. 
In the absence of complete data from one environment, the principles of 
mineral nutrient cycling in such a system are illustrated by the growing of 
winter wheat in monoculture using the national averages for yield of grain 
and quantities of fertilizer used. 
Timetable for system 
Sow in October/November adding a small part of the nitrogen fertilizer 
and most or all of the Ρ and K; roll and top dress with further nitrogen in 
the spring; harvest in August; burn stubble and prepare seed bed using 
minimum cultivation or direct drilling. 
6.2.6. Nutrient balances in contrasted U.K. systems 
In all our systems we have assumed that plant and animal compartments 
(pools) are in a steady state, and we have used this assumption to calculate 
one closing (or balancing) entry in each section. 
It has been further assumed that both the total soil compartment and its 
constituent pools (A, B, C) are not necessarily in a steady state, and gains or 
losses have been calculated from the available data. These are at best only 
rough estimates because they represent the accumulated errors arising from 
assumptions and calculations made in deriving values for all the fluxes. Where 
unknown (pm) quantities are involved — particularly regarding mineralization 
^ immobilization of organic matter, and weathering ^ fixation by soil miner­
als — the calculated gains or losses are particularly doubtful. 
Bearing these qualifications in mind, we have attempted to draw some con­
clusions regarding the possible changes in the soil pools of the systems we 
have described. 
High elevation moorland 
The total soil component appears to be close to steady state with respect to 
Ν and P, and the apparent loss of Κ may be made good by the release from 
weathering. 
The soil organic pool appears to be gaining Ν, Ρ and Κ at the expense of 
the available soil nutrient pool. While the steady accumulation of organic 
matter in litter layers and peat in such soils is possible, it cannot proceed in­
definitely. It is probable that net mineralization is greater than indicated in

I. 
It is assumed that finishing' lambs are fed 0.45 kg concentrate feed per day for 21 days 
and that each pregnant ewe eats 51 kg y"
1 of the same feed; the concentration of nutrients 
in the feed was taken to be 3.3% N , 0.16% Ρ and 1.1% K. 
4. 
Herbage production, 5750 kg of dry matter per ha (4250 without fertilizer, plus 
120 X 12.5 = 1500 following the application of nitrogen fertilizer). It is assumed that 15% 
dry matter is wasted or spoiled in the grazing process and that 25% dry matter is wasted 
in making and feeding silage. On these assumptions the overall utilisation of available 
herbage was taken to be 81%. 
The concentration of nutrients in grass/clover silage was taken to be 3.5% N , 0.4% P, 
2.8% K, and in the freshly grazed grass/clover 3.2% N , 0.35% Ρ and 2.5% Κ (Spedding and 
Diekmanns, 1972). 
5. The system on this pasture type is operated to the schedule described previously 
(Table 11). It was assumed that the lambs were sold weighing 45 kg each, and that the 
nutrients in the bought-in replacement hoggs balance those in the sold-off cast ewes. 
The concentrations of nutrients in the lamb carcass were taken as 2.8% N , 0.65% Ρ 
and 0.18% Κ (Sykes and Field, 1972). 
It is assumed that each ewe produces 2 kg wool containing 17.8% Ν (Ryder and 
Stephenson, 1968). 
8, 9. The nutrients are apportioned between faeces and urine in the same manner as that 
described in ref. Newbould and Floate-2 to -5. 
I I . Fertilizer amounts are based on national average levels (Church, 1975) and on recom­
mendations of the Edinburgh School of Agriculture (1969). 
12. Nitrogen fixed by clover in the U.K. is reported to vary from 23 to 400 kg ha"
1 by 
Nutman (1971) and from 105 to 430 kg ha"
1 by Whitehead (1970). An approximate mean 
value of 150 kg ha"
1 was used. 
15. Inputs in rain are based on Cooke (1976). 
16, 17. The net quantities of Κ released by weathering, and of nitrogen and Ρ mineralized 
from organic matter are based on uptake by unfertilized leys on the reference plots at 
Rothamsted (Widdowson and Penny, 1973). 
21. Leaching loss for nitrogen is based on Low (1973), for Ρ and Κ on Cooke (1976). Use 
of the latter data may underestimate Κ loss; Wilkinson and Lowrey (1973) quote values 
up to 139 kg ha"
1. 
24. 71% of Ρ and 46% of Κ in applied fertilizer is assumed to add to the mineral soil frac­
tion (Widdowson and Penny, 1973). 
25. System is assumed to be gaining in organic matter and hence Ν and P, but amounts 
immobilised by the soil organic fraction are not known. 
26a. It is assumed that all Κ enters the pool of available soil nutrients. 
26b. It is assumed that Ν and Ρ remain in the soil organic matter pool.

NOTES TO T A B L E 14. 
(Reference Newbould and Floate-9) 
11. Fertilizer amounts are national average levels used on winter wheat (Church, 1975). At 
Rothamsted, from which other data for the balance on this crop were obtained, the 
amounts of fertilizer Ν , Ρ and Κ used annually on arable crops, including winter wheat, 
over a period of 15 years were, on average, 70—118 N , 27 Ρ and 104—208 Κ (kg ha"
1) 
(Widdowson and Penny, 1973). The Ν and Ρ levels used at Rothamsted are similar to the 
national averages but the amount of Κ is very much greater.

18. The yield of grain was taken as 4356 kg ha"
1, i.e. the average yield for the U.K. crop 
in 1973/1974 (Annual Review of Agriculture, 1975). The contents of nutrients used for

(Halliday, 1948). 
20. Yield of straw was assumed to be similar to the yield of grain and it was assumed that 
straw was burnt in situ, resulting mainly in a loss of nitrogen. 
21. Leaching losses based on mean data for Rothamsted 1970—1974. Ranges Ν (3.85— 
82.3), Ρ (<0.05-1.25), Κ (4.6—40), (Williams, 1976). Data for Saxmundham (heavy clay/ 
good wheat land) indicated that leaching losses would be 22.6 N , 0.02 Ρ and 1.2 Κ kg ha"
1 
assuming 100 mm drainage through the profile (Cooke, 1976). 
24. Phosphorus and potassium * fixed' in soil were estimated at 71% and 46% of that applied 
in fertilizer (Widdowson and Penny, 1973). 
25. A steady state in soil organic matter and a balance of mineralization and immobilization 
of Ν and Ρ were assumed. 
26a. It was assumed that all Κ enters the pool of available soil nutrients ( A ) . 
26b. It was assumed that Ν and Ρ remain in the soil organic matter pool (B). 
the tables, and it is also possible that intermittent peat erosion keeps the 
system in a steady state over a long time scale. 
U.K. traditional hill sheep farming 
Like the high elevation moorland system, this system appears to be close 
to a steady state in the total soil component. A small gain in Ν contrasts with 
a small loss in the previous system, but the difference is probably not signif­
icant and is certainly less than the errors associated with some of the fluxes. 
The apparent gains in the soil organic pool of this system, which are almost 
matched by apparent losses from the available soil nutrient pool, can probably 
be explained in the same way as for the high elevation moorland system. In 
this case though, the underestimate of net mineralization is more likely than 
the intermittent erosion of peat.

--- Page 67 ---

64 
Soil organic matter levels under perennial grassland are well known to be 
remarkably constant for long periods, so that values close to steady state would 
be expected for this system. 
U.K. improved hill sheep farming 
The analysis of this system consists of two sub-systems and an attempted 
synthesis for the whole farm system. We shall consider first the whole farm 
system followed by any possible interactions between the sub-systems. 
The total soil component appears to be gaining Ν and Ρ but losing K. The 
small gain in Ν may not be significant for reasons discussed under the previous 
system. The gain in Ρ is probably real and is due to fertilizer addition which 
is only balanced by small losses in animal product and by leaching. The 
apparent Κ loss may be made good by release through weathering. 
The apparent gains in the soil organic pool are probably too large for 
reasons already discussed, and for the same reasons the apparent losses from 
the available soil nutrient pool are probably erroneously large. The gain in Ρ 
originates from fertilizer addition. 
The gain in Ν in the total soil pool of the improved paddocks may be at 
the expense of loss from the hill grasslands, but care must be taken in the 
interpretation of the data because of the different sizes of the two sub-systems. 
Hill grassland is close to steady state with respect to P, while the improved 
paddocks have gained as a result of fertilizer addition. The reasons for the 
apparent Κ losses have already been discussed. 
The soil organic pools of both sub-systems appear to be gaining in Ν, Ρ and 
Κ but, especially where lime has been applied to the improved paddocks, 
mineralization must be greater than indicated in the tables. In the hill grassland 
sub-system the gains in the organic pool are almost matched by the losses 
from the available pool. The large gains in Ν and Κ in the organic pool of the 
improved paddocks are unlikely to be real but there could be a small conver­
sion of applied fertilizer Ρ to organic form. 
The balances for this system suggest that in the absence of organic return 
via faeces, the available pool of Ν will be depleted — it is, however, more than 
replenished in the long term as faecal Ν is mineralized. 
Application of phosphorus fertilizer on a 5-year cycle should more than 
make good the losses unless fixation is high, which it may be in some soils. 
Losses of Κ appear to exceed gains unless there is a significant release from 
weathering or unless some losses have been overestimated. 
It is interesting to note that, compared with the traditional system, an in­
crease of 60—80% in cycling minerals leads to a lamb production increase of 
over 100% (see Table 15). 
Woodland 
The high positive balance for nitrogen is much influenced by the excep­
tionally high figure for nitrogen fixation. However, the wilderness area on 
Broadbalk at Rothamsted is apparently gaining significant amounts of nitrogen

Intensive arable — winter wheat 
The total soil compartment appears to gain nitrogen and phosphorus and 
to lose potassium. The gain in nitrogen is 'apparent' only because no estimate 
for denitrification is possible. In the arable rotation of the Rothamsted 
(1956—1970) and Woburn reference plots (1960—1969) nitrogen was gained 
only when farmyard manure was used (Williams, 1973). The gain in phos­
phorus is probably genuine and it was observed at both Rothamsted and 
Woburn. The loss in potassium despite the addition of potassium fertilizer 
is unexpected by comparison with the observed data, although losses in this 
element did occur at Woburn when nitrogen and phosphorus fertilizers were 
added. The observation of potassium loss may be a consequence of the 
method of calculating the data and may indicate use of too low a level of 
nitrogen fertilizer in the calculation (see note 11). Changes in the pool of 
available soil nutrients are not unexpected in view of the previous discussion, 
but it should be noted that the apparently large loss of phosphorus is more 
than balanced by a gain in the mineral soil compartment. 
Intensive sheep — grass/clover and grass 
The balances for the pool of available soil nutrients in both systems show 
large gains in Ν and Κ and small losses of P. However, since the losses of 
nitrogen by denitrification and volatilization are not known, the gain for this

where all three elements show gains. The gain in phosphorus is probably 
genuine because of the very small amount of the element removed in the 
animal product. This contrasts with the arable system where considerable 
amounts of phosphorus are removed in the primary product.

6.2.7. Summary table and discussion of U.K. systems 
Seven different ecosystems in the U.K. have been discussed in the preceding 
section and, of these, six are concerned with agricultural production. In 
summary it is useful to compare the amounts of nutrients in circulation in 
these systems and to consider the gains and losses in the soil compartment. 
For this purpose the systems are listed in Tables 16 and 17 in increasing order 
of amounts of nutrients annually entering and leaving the soil compartment, 
and it is instructive to note that this order more or less corresponds with the 
increasing order of sophistication based on production and degree of manipula­
tion of the system. 
Annual turnover and production are lowest in the moorland system (New­
bould and Floate-1) where climatic limitations are severe. It is likely that 
climatic limitations are imposed upon both plant nutrient uptake and release 
from decomposing organic matter, and the system is probably close to steady

--- Page 71 ---

68 
state. The small changes in balance are well within the limits of accuracy with 
which the various flows are known; the unknown amount of release from the 
weathering of rocks probably accounts for the apparent loss of K. 
Traditional hill sheep farming systems (Newbould and Floate-2) also have 
a small annual turnover of nutrients. This is also in part owing to climatic 
limitations to plant production and decomposition (although less severe than 
in the moorland system) but also in part owing to the low level of utilisation 
of herbage production. Only a small part of the nutrients taken up by grass­
land is consumed by grazing animals and the remainder returns to the soil 
organic pool. 
Intensification of the same system (Newbould and Floate-3), involving 
land improvement of a small part and increased stocking and herbage utilisa­
tion overall, leads to a 60—80% increase in nutrient turnover, and to a doubling 
of production. In both the traditional and improved versions of the system 
the changes in soil nutrient balances are small and within the limits of accuracy 
of the data, suggesting that the whole system is near equilibrium. 
The data for the woodland system (Newbould and Floate-6) indicate 
rather large amounts of nutrients in circulation compared with the other 
natural or semi-natural systems which do not include fertilizer inputs. This 
may in part be due to considerably moderated climatic limitations to produc­
tion and decomposition at this lowland site and in part to the deeper rooting 
habit of the trees on a more fertile soil. White and Harrison (in preparation) 
do, however, point out that in comparison with other woodland and forest 
studies the values for their nutrient data are above average, and that this 
appears inconsistent with the fact that the site is described as commercially 
non-productive woodland. 
Apart from the very small overall addition of Ρ in system 2.2b, all the 
foregoing systems rely on recycling of nutrients within the systems and are 
thus in marked contrast to the more intensive agricultural production systems. 
The intensive arable (winter wheat) system (Newbould and Floate-9) involves 
application of all three major nutrients in significant amounts, and this in large 
part explains the increased turnover of nutrients in this system. However, it 
should also be pointed out that in contrast to the animal production systems, 
all nutrients taken up by the aerial parts of the crop appear in the balance 
sheet. There does appear to be a significant gain in soil-N, but real changes are 
only detected in soil-N when there is a major change in the management of 
the system as, for example, from arable to grassland or vice-versa. It is therefore 
concluded that the apparent gain is probably accounted for by unidentified 
losses, most probably via the gaseous phase or by leaching below drain depth. 
The balance for Ρ is near to steady state but Κ is apparently in deficit. 
This Κ deficit may be owing to our use of values which are too low for fer­
tilizer (1) or release from rocks by weathering (16) and/or too high for 
leaching (21) or fixation' (24). Alternatively, it may reflect our attempts to 
select the best available data which, unfortunately, come from a wide variety 
of sources. Had other combinations of data from the same sources been used,

--- Page 72 ---

69 
Κ would have been more nearly in balance, but the balances for the other 
nutrients would have been disturbed. This strikingly illustrates the need to 
strive for the ideal of fully integrated measurements collected for each par­
ticular system. 
The intensive grassland systems (Newbould and Floate-7 and -8) involve 
significant additions of nutrients as fertilizer and particularly high amounts 
of Ν on the all-grass system (N and F1.-8). These large amounts of applied Ν 
are reflected in the apparent soil-N gains, but again such increases are seldom 
detected in measured changes in soil-N. Indeed, soil-N levels remain remarkably 
constant for long periods under grassland (Richardson, 1938) and significant 
changes in soil-N are only measurable after 5—10 years with intensive use of 
fertilizers (Clement, 1961; Clement and Williams, 1964). It is assumed that 
these apparent gains are really unidentified losses, probably in the gaseous 
form. These apparent losses highlight an important gap in existing knowledge, 
which will not be filled until more reliable data are available for the movement 
of Ν into drainage water below field drain level, and for gaseous losses by

denitrification, and volatilization from organic matter and animal excreta. 
Similar conclusions were reached by Beek and Frissel (1973) and it is note­
worthy that even the careful gas lysimeter studies of Henzell and his co-workers 
(Martin and Ross, 1968; Ross et al., 1968) have not solved the enigma of the 
soil-N balance which was reviewed in detail by Allison (1955, 1966). It is 
noteworthy that such losses appear to be significantly greater in these animal-
based systems than in the intensive arable system, suggesting that a major source 
of N-loss may be by way of animal excreta. 
Data for nitrogen balances for intensively managed grassland experiments at 
Jealott's Hill (England) have recently become available (Hood, 1976) and these 
are summarised in Table 18. These data confirm both the very large Ν losses 
from such systems and our conclusions regarding the possible sources of such 
losses. 
The soils of these intensive animal production systems seem to be gaining 
significant quantities of Ρ and K, provided that the Ρ in the faeces is included 
in the balance. If the amounts released by mineralization of organic matter, 
and by weathering, were known more accurately, the gain might be even 
greater and might suggest that some economy of fertilizer use could be made 
in such systems. In this context it is interesting to note that Adams and 
McAllister (1975) have recently suggested a novel basis of calculating Ρ and Κ 
fertilizer requirements on the livestock farm based on the nutrient output in 
the excreta per animal-unit carried by the system. 
6.3.1. AGRO-ECOSYSTEMS IN THE N E T H E R L A N D S , P A R T I (J. Damen) 
A mixed livestock farm in the south-western clay district of The Netherlands 
in 1800 
To compare present-day intensive systems with an old extensive system 
from the same area, a self-sustaining farm such as existed around the year 
1800 was analyzed. 
Classification. Three nutrient balances were prepared, one for the arable part, 
one for the livestock part, and one for the farm as a whole. The reference 
numbers are: 
Livestock part of mixed (self-sustaining) system. Reference: Damen-1; live­
stock part of Dutch farm of 1800, Table 20. 
Arable part of mixed (self-sustaining) system. Reference: Damen-2; arable 
part of Dutch farm of 1800, Table 21. 
Mixed (self-sustaining) system. Reference: Damen-3: Dutch mixed livestock 
farm of 1800, ratio arable area: livestock area = 1 : 1 , Table 22. 
Nutrient input took place mainly by rain and N2-fixation, while nutrients 
left the system by leaching, volatilization from soil organic matter and ma-

nure, and by the sale of a part of the harvest products, including milk and 
meat. The balance given in Table 19 was set up using an energy balance of an 
arable farm around 1800, with data derived by Dekkers et al. (1974) based 
on a study of Baars (1973). 
This was a farm of 55 ha, of which less than half of the surface was used to 
grow marketable products. The remaining part was mainly used for growing 
food for cattle and horses and nearly 10% of the surface was fallowed, mainly 
for weed control. The cattle and horses were a source of manure and power 
and produced some milk and meat as by-products. Nearly two-thirds of the 
primary production (grass, clover, fodder, straw) was used for this purpose. 
Apart from this, a considerable part of the other products (grains, potatoes, 
beans) were used for maintaining the labour force of about 10 persons and 
for seed.

78 
By using the Dutch handbook for agricultural information, these yield data 
have been translated, as far as possible, into nutrient data. 
There are two ways to regard a farm system like this: 
— The farm is split up into two separate ecosystems, an arable one and a live­
stock one. 
— The farm is regarded as being one ecosystem. 
The first way is more applicable to the farming structure of those days. From 
the data it appears that in the past the arable ecosystem was enriched with 
nutrients via dunging with manure, while the livestock ecosystem was exhaust­
ed by harvesting clover and grasses without any dunging. 
Thus, three nutrient balances can be calculated, which are shown in Tables 
20—22. In order to find the data for these balances and to make the tables 
comparable, some assumptions had to be made: 
— The arable part of the farm is equal to the livestock part, namely 25 ha. 
The fallowed part is not taken into account. 
— The percentage of nutrients in the crop species has not changed appreciably 
from 1800 till now. 
— The quantity of seed used per ha was the same around 1800 as it is today. 
— According to Becking (personal communication, 1976) the following fig­
ures are used for N2-fixation: 
beans 
200 kg Ν ha"
1 
clover 
(200)-250 kg Ν ha"
1 
peas 
150-200 kg Ν ha"
1 
— Figures for input by rain and output by leaching have been taken from 
Henkens (see next section). 
— The ratio between summer and winter consumption of grasses and clover 
by the cattle amounts to 0.55:0.45 (derived from Henkens, see next sec­
tion). 
— The ratio between available and not directly available manure is assumed 
to be 0.7:0.3. 
The human consumption of beans, potatoes and grains on the farm itself 
is estimated from the labour mentioned in the costs column of Table 19, 
using arbitrary ratios between these products. 
A D D I T I O N A L NOTES TO TABLES 20-22. 
(References Damen-1 to -3) 
Because the figures for manure production by animals and men are the most uncertain, 
this item is used to close the balance of the livestock part of the table. Losses from ma­
nure to air are assumed to be 20%. As can be seen in Table 20, separation of the farm into 
two systems gives an exhaustion of the livestock part except for nitrogen, because of the 
N 2 -fixation of clover, and an enrichment of the arable part. When considering the farm as 
one ecosystem, losses and supplies should be in equilibrium. By taking into account the 
inaccuracy of the calculations, the figures in Table 22 can be regarded as confirming this 
equilibrium. 
For further details on soil type and climate the reader is referred to the next section by 
Henkens, who described the same area, but for the situation at the present day.

--- Page 82 ---

79 
6.3.2. AGRO-ECOSYSTEMS IN THE N E T H E R L A N D S , PART II (Ch.H. Henkens) 
For the description of nutrient cycling in Dutch agricultural ecosystems, 
generalisations are inevitable. With regard to the ecosystems, two main groups 
can be distinguished: marine clay soils and sandy soils. The subdivisions of 
these two groups are based on type of land use; i.e. grassland and arable land. 
A distinction has also been made between the production during the period 
just before 1940 and during the past few years. Differences in yield between 
these periods result mainly from the use of fertilizers and concentrates, 
which markedly affect the nutrient balances. 
Description of the clay area 
Location. This system is located partly in the north-east, partly in the south­
west of The Netherlands. The altitude ranges from —6 below to 10 m above 
sea level. Coordinates are approx. 53.3° N, 6.5° Ε and 51.5° N, 4° E, respec­
tively. 
Climate. Both areas have a maritime climate with a mean temperature of 9°C 
and an average annual precipitation (P) of 75 cm. According to Penman 
(1956), the annual average amount of potential evaporation (E 0) is 75 cm, 
which would lead to an average annual surplus of precipitation of 22.5 cm 
(E = 0.7 E 0). In fact the water surplus is a little higher (see water regime). 
The precipitation is reasonably spread over the year, with a minimum from 
March to June and relatively high values from July to August. Usually there 
is an evaporation deficit from April to July. 
Soil. Geologically this area consists of young marine clay deposits. Soil types 
are: young clay soils, calcareous and slightly calcareous moderately sandy 
and light clay, humus-poor and shallowly humose. 
Water regime. Being very close to sea level, almost all clay soils are polders, 
so that the phreatic level is controlled, often at a depth of 100—120 cm. 
Drainage can be estimated at 25—30 cm, depending on the type of vegetation. 
During dry periods, the soil water storage is sufficiently high most of the time 
to counteract the precipitation deficit. 
Use. Both areas are used mainly for arable farming. However, the crop rota­
tion system differs. In the north-east, a crop rotation of wheat, beets, wheat, 
barley, beets and wheat again is used. In the south-west, the scheme is: peas, 
potatoes, wheat, flax, beets and barley. Differences in nutrient cycling are 
only slight, so that only mean values for the whole system are given. A dis­
tinction is made between areas from which the tops and leaves of beets are 
sold and areas where these products are ploughed in.

--- Page 83 ---

80 
In part of the older polders, the phreatic level is considerably higher than 
— 100 cm. These soils are so wet that arable farming is difficult and they are 
used for dairy cows. The removal of nutrients in the form of milk is high, and 
losses are made good by fertilizers or purchased concentrates. During the last 
20 years, both systems have been used within one farm: 2.5 cows ha"
1 is, 
therefore, quite common, and in exceptional cases 4 cows ha"
1 are main­
tained. 
Description of the sandy area 
Location. This system is situated in the north-eastern part of The Netherlands, 
in south-east Drenthe. The coordinates are 52.8° Ν and 6.6° E. The altitude 
ranges from 20 to 30 m above sea level. 
Climate. A maritime climate with a mean annual temperature of 9°C. The 
average annual precipitation and potential evaporation amount to 75 and 68 
cm, respectively. This leads to a precipitation surplus of 28 cm. 
Geology and soil. Geologically this area lies on a ridge probably formed by a 
tectonic movement. The soil consists of non-loamy, occasionally slightly 
loamy sand and is poor in humus. 
Use. Although the common land use is mixed livestock farming, a rather pure 
form of arable farming is carried out in addition. Pure dairy farming is not 
found on this type of soil. 
Grassland farms 
Classification. Four nutrient balance tables, all for the same area, but based 
on increasing amounts of fertilizers and supplemental feed, are described. 
Because neither fertilizers nor supplemental feed are used in the first system 
described, this system is considered by the editor as an extensive one. 
Extensive livestock system. Reference: Henkens-1; Dutch dairy farm, clay 
soil, 1937; no fertilizers or supplemental feed. Milk production 2924 1 ha
- 1, 
meat 145 kg ha"
1, Table 23. 
Intensive livestock system. Reference: Henkens-2; Dutch dairy farm, clay 
soil, 1937; 0.86 cows ha"
1, no fertilizers, with supplemental feed, milk pro­
duction 3800 kg, meat production 145 kg ha"
1, Table 24. 
Intensive livestock system. Reference: Henkens-3; Dutch dairy farm, clay 
soil, 1972; 2.5 cows ha"
1, with fertilizers and supplemental feed, milk pro­
duction 11 250 kg ha"
1, meat 480 kg ha"
1, Table 25. 
Intensive livestock system. Reference: Henkens-4; Dutch dairy farm, clay

--- Page 84 ---

81 
Special attention is given to the method of calculating the cow density per 
ha in a livestock farming system, without the use of any arable land, and with­
out supplemental feed, in this section called "concentrates". The density of 
cows per ha was derived by estimating the starch equivalent (s.e.) requirement 
of the animals, given a certain production level of milk and meat (Anonymus, 
1973b). 
Maintenance requirement in kg s.e. per day 
= 1 + (3.33 X weight)/1000 
Requirement for 1 kg milk (fat content 3.2%) = 0.252 kg s.e. 
Requirement for 1 kg milk (fat content 4.0%) = 0.286 kg s.e. 
Requirement within the dry period (60 days) = requirement of 5 kg milk 
day
- 1 in 1930 
= requirement of 10 kg milk 
day"
1 in 1970 
Requirement for calves aged 3—6 months 
= 2.200 kg s.e. 
Requirement for calves aged > 1 year 
= 2.800 kg s.e. 
Before 1940 the net production of grassland in the absence of fertilizers 
was 2200 kg s.e. The milk production at that time was 3400 kg with a fat 
content of 3.2%. Each cow (500 kg) was accompanied by 0.39 calf aged 3—6 
months and 0.32 calf > 1 year. This leads to an annual requirement per cow 
plus accompanying young animals of 2546 kg s.e. Thus, farms in the thirties 
could afford a cattle density of 0.86 dairy cow per ha together with accompa­
nying young cattle. 
This cattle density is in agreement with that at the beginning of this cen­
tury in The Netherlands (De Boer, 1975). Nowadays there may still be areas 
where no fertilizers are used. Nevertheless, the farmer will like a higher pro­
duction. This higher production can only be attained by using concentrates. 
Tables 23 and 24 show the nutrient balances of both farm types. 
In the seventies the production of the milking cows is 4500 kg milk with 
4% fat. The weight of a cow is 550 kg. Each cow is accompanied by 0.41 calf 
age < 1 year and 0.44 calf > 1 year. Using 400 kg Ν as fertilizer the net pro­
duction is 4500 kg s.e. (Anonymus, 1975). This leads to an annual require­
ment per cow plus accompanying young animals of 3272 kg s.e., which means 
that a cattle density of 1.37 cow per ha, together with accompanying young 
cattle is possible. Of course, higher densities are achieved by using concen­
trates. 
Tables 25 and 26 show the nutrient balances of two farms using 400 kg Ν 
ha"
1 y"
1; one with a density of 2.5 cows + accompanying young cattle, the 
other with a density of 4 cows + accompanying young cattle. 
For the farm with 2.5 cows per ha on marine clay soil the method of calcu­
lation is given. Some remarks should be made about the other calculations. 
(1) On the farm without fertilizers the net production (= food value taken 
up by the animals) will, because of lower cattle density, be higher than on 
intensive farms. For Tables 23 and 24 we assumed a utilization of 70% of the 
grass production. For Tables 25 and 26 we assumed a utilization of 60% of 
the grass production.

6m. Manure production in winter

In meat and milk (45% X item 5) 
32 kg N ; 6 kg P; 
8 kg Κ 
In manure 
162 kg N ; 26 kg P; 127 kg Κ 
Part of the nitrogen in manure is mineral; part is organic. 
Part of the organic nitrogen is available after a short time (Nf); part becomes available 
later ( N r) -> soil organic matter. 
Thus, N t = N
m + N f + N r 
N m = 0.4 N t ; N f = 0.3 N t ; N r = 0.3 N t (Anonymus, 1976) 
Part of the N
m will be lost to the air before it reaches the soil. This is ± 20%. Thus, 
Lost from manure to air: 0.2 X 0.4 N t = 0.08 N t = 13 kg Ν 
8a. The rest of the N
m will be available, whereas the Nf will become available during the 
year; the N r will become available in the following years. Thus, 
Manure from own farm: 0.8 N
m + N f = 0.32 N t + 0.3 N t = 100 kg N ; 26 kg P; 127 kg Κ 
8b. Manure from own farm: N r = 0 . 3 N t = 
49 kg Ν 
8. Manure applied to own farm: 8a + 8b = 149 kg N ; 26 kg P; 127 kg Κ 
6d. Droppings on grazed areas 
Uptake by grazing 
144 kg Ν ; 18 kg P; 120 kg Κ 
Uptake from concentrates ( C s) 
90 kg N ; 21 kg P; 40 kg Κ

234 kg N ; 39 kg P; 160 kg Κ

40 kg N ; 8 kg P; 10 kg Κ

Before the droppings reach the soil ± 20% of N
m will be lost to the air. Thus, 
Lost from droppings to air: 
0.2 X 0.4 N t = 0.08 N t = 16 kg Ν

N r = 0.3 Nf. 
= 58 kg Ν

20. On grassland, manure and droppings are not ploughed in. Lying on top of the soil 
± 20% of the N
m from items 8 and 9 will be lost by volatilization. Thus,

m = 0.08 N t (item 8) = 12 kg Ν 
from droppings (20d)

11. Based on Anonymus, 1976, the net s.e. production of 4500 kg ha"
1 can be achieved 
by fertilizing with 400 kg Ν per ha. Fertilizing with Ρ and Κ is not needed, keeping the 
mentioned number of animals. Thus, 
Fertilizers: 400 kg N ; 0 kg P; 0 kg Κ 
12. At this nitrogen level N-fixation is negligible. 
15. Between 1 October 1973 and 1 October 1974 the rain was collected in 14 different 
places scattered over the country, and was analysed for N.P.K. (Henkens, 1976). 
Rain: 
14 kg N ; 1.0 kg P; 5 kg Κ 
21. Henkens (1972, 1976) mentions some analytical data for drainage water on grassland, 
thus: 
Leaching: 
39 kg N ; 0.24 kg P; 11 kg Κ 
26b. In item 26 the plant products remaining on the field are mentioned. This organic 
material will build up soil humus. N o figures are known about this building up on 
grassland. Kolenbrander (1974) mentioned that after one year 20 kg humus is built up 
from 100 kg d.m. plant foliage. This means that from the 6000 kg d.m. remaining on the 
field, 1200 kg humus is produced. We assume a nitrogen content of 4%. Thus, 
Plant products remaining on the field: 
48 kg Ν 
26a. The rest of the nitrogen from 26 and the Ρ and Κ will become available. Thus, 
Plant products remaining on the field: 
132 kg N ; 23 kg P; 150 kg Κ 
19. Dilz and Woldendorp (1960) came to the conclusion that on clay soils 22% of the 
applied nitrogen is lost. As leaching had been excluded, these losses must be attributed 
to denitrification. Therefore we assume that 22% of the available nitrogen will be lost 
by denitrification, thus: 
Denitrification: 22% of (26a + 8a + 9a + 11 + 15) = 0.22 X 766 = 169 kg Ν 
(2) On a farm with 4 cows + accompanying young animals the grazing is 
very intense, so only a small part of the grass can be cut. According to Lam-
mers (1973) only 0.2 cutting per ha is possible here. 
(3) In the table for the 2.5-cow farm, a leaching of 39 kg Ν and 11 kg Κ is 
mentioned. This is 5% of the Ν and 3% of the Κ in the pool of available soil 
nutrients. For the other farms the same percentage of leaching is assumed. 
(4) There are no tables given for sandy soils. Only two items are different, 
i.e. leaching and denitrification. The leaching of Κ on sandy soil is 15% of the 
Κ in the pool of available soil nutrients. There is no difference in leaching of 
Ν and Ρ between clay and sandy grassland. Denitrification on sandy grassland 
is 16% instead of the 22% on clay soils (Dilz and Woldendorp, 1960).

Arable farming 
Classification. Two almost identical crop rotation systems are compared to 
each other; one in which the tops of beets are removed and another one in 
which the tops are ploughed in. 
Intensive arable system. Reference: Henkens-5; Dutch arable farm, clay soil, 
crop rotation, beet tops ploughed in, Table 28. 
Intensive arable system. Reference: Henkens-6; Dutch arable farm, clay soil, 
crop rotation, beet tops removed, Table 29. 
On an arable farm different crops are grown, and so the mean annual 
removal should be based on a rotation. The following is taken as a crop rota­
tion: potatoes, wheat, sugar beets, wheat. This is a frequent rotation in The 
Netherlands. Table 27 shows the yields and the nutrient contents. The yield 
hardly influences the nutrient content and so the removal is determined by 
yield. The same table shows the quantity of crop residues expressed in dry 
matter (Anonymus, 1973a). 
A distinction has been made between areas from which tops and leaves of 
sugar beets are sold and areas where these products are ploughed in.

Tables 28 and 29 give the nutrient flow in kg ha"
1 y"
1 of crop land as a 
mean of the rotations mentioned, on a marine clay soil. 
The nitrogen flow requires further discussion. The harvested part of the 
crops (beet tops and leaves included) take up 166 kg N. According to the 
results of field experiments, 50% of the nitrogen given is stored in the harvest­
ed crop (Sluysmans, 1966). Thus 332 kg Ν fertilizer is needed, the other 50% 
being stored in the plant products remaining on the field or disappearing by 
leaching or denitrification and volatilization. According to the results of Van 
der Paauw and Ris (1963) 13.5% of the nitrogen, which is not taken up by 
the harvested crop, will be available in the following year. This after-effect 
depends on the rainfall. This means that 0.135 X 166 = 22 kg Ν will remain 
in the soil.

--- Page 100 ---

97 
In the case where the beet tops and leaves are removed, 3500 kg dry matter 
containing 29 kg Ν remains on the fields. According to Kolenbrander (1974), 
30—35 kg humus will be formed after one year from 100 kg dry matter of 
plant residues. This means that from 3500 kg dry matter of plant residues 
0.32 X 3500 kg = 1120 kg humus (4% N ) will be formed, containing 45 kg N. 
This means that 45 — 29 = 16 kg of additional Ν is bound by the new humus. 
These 16 kg Ν are obtained from the fertilizer nitrogen or from the nitrogen 
delivered by breakdown of soil humus. We will assume that there is equilibri­
um between breakdown and building up of humus in our examples. Thus the 
breakdown of soil humus will yield 45 kg N, so that the net result is an en­
richment of available nitrogen in the soil of 29 kg N. 
In the case where the sugar beet tops and leaves are ploughed in, an extra 
amount of 5250/4 kg dry matter per year containing 40 kg Ν is supplied to 
the soil. From this material 0.25 X 1312 = 328 kg humus, containing 13 kg N, 
will be formed after one year. Thus, by ploughing in the tops and leaves from 
sugar beets an extra 40 — 13 = 27 kg available Ν is supplied. This amount 
should be subtracted from the Ν fertilizer needed so that, when beet tops are 
ploughed in, 305 kg Ν fertilizer is needed. Of course, in the year directly 
after sugar beets, more nitrogen can be subtracted. 
Based on analytical data (Henkens, 1972, 1976) of drainage water on crop 
land, the leaching (300 mm drainage water) is: 
on marine clay soils: 
58 kg N; 
0.2 kg P; 
40 kg Κ 
on sandy soils: 
85 kg N; 
0.06 kg P; 
48 kg Κ 
With the rain water, 14 kg N ; 1.0 kg P; and 5 kg Κ are supplied (Henkens, 
1976). Thus, in fact, only 44 kg of the Ν fertilizer is leached on marine clay 
soil and 71 kg on sandy soil. From the 332 kg Ν fertilizer 166 + 22 + 29 + 44 = 
261 kg Ν on marine clay soil is now accounted for and on sandy soil 166 + 22 + 
24 + 61 = 273 kg N. The rest, 71 or 59 kg respectively, is not accounted for. 
This amount is lost by denitrification. 
6.4. AGRO-ECOSYSTEMS IN G E R M A N Y (B. Ulrich) 
Forests in Central Europe 
Three forest systems have been described. 
Classification. 
Forest. Reference: Ulrich-1; Deciduous and coniferous forests, acid soils, 
northern hemisphere, Table 31. 
Forest. Reference: Ulrich-2; Coniferous forest on grey-brown podsolic soil, 
Central Europe (IBP-Solling project), Table 32. 
Forest. Reference: Ulrich-3; Deciduous forest on grey-brown podsolic soil, 
Central Europe (IBP-Solling project), Table 33. 
For forests, some special aspects have to be considered with respect to nutri­
ent cycling.

--- Page 101 ---

98 
6.4.1. Input 
Due to their larger extension into the troposphere (tree height 30—40 m), 
forests have an increased input from the atmosphere by interception of air 
constituents. Air masses exchanging between the crown space and the 
boundary layer of the troposphere carry aerosols as well as gases to the leaf 
surfaces, where they can be adsorbed or dissolved after impaction. The 
amount of this additional input by interception depends upon the air im­
purities, and in forest regions of Central Europe is approximately 5 kg N, 0.1 
kg Ρ and 7 kg Κ per ha and per year. The removal of impurities from the air 
is, on the one hand, a special contribution of forests to air cleansing, and on 
the other hand, an appreciable contribution to the nutrient requirement, 
accounting for approximately 8% of Ν uptake, 2% of Ρ uptake and 30% of 
Κ uptake. 
6.4.2. Uptake 
In contrast to agricultural crops, it is easily possible in a forest to measure 
and collect the precipitation after passage through the leaf space. Measure­
ments of this kind show that the rain leaches potassium out of the leaves in 
an amount comparable to the net uptake, calculated as the sum of forest 
increment and litter fall. No leaf leaching occurs in the oases of Ν and P, 
at least at the low nutrient levels encountered in acid forest soils. Leaf leach­
ing occurs very probably in agricultural crops too. Its neglect is not impor­
tant in a consideration of annual balances, but it should be taken into ac­
count in simulation models because it increases the rate of turnover. 
6.4.3. Primary products sold 
Forests differ from agricultural crops in the time span between two har­
vests. In a forest with a uniform age structure, approximately half of the 
annual increment is harvested over a time interval of 15—30 years; the other 
half is allowed to accumulate on the site and is harvested at the end of the 
life span of the stand. For ease of comparison, the amounts listed under 
"primary products sold" correspond to the total mean annual increment, 
irrespective of the time of harvesting. 
At a given harvesting time, only part of the accumulated increment m^y 
be taken out of the forest. For example, the bole may be exported, whereas the 
bark and the branches remain on the forest floor, becoming part of the plant 
products remaining on the field. Since modern forestry tends to whole-tree 
logging, the annual increment was considered to belong in total to the pri­
mary products sold. 
6.4.4. Changes in soil component 
Forest stands of a uniform age may have life spans between 80 years 
(Douglas fir, Norway spruce) and 240 years (oak). The final harvest (clear 
cut) means a destruction of the ecosystem, especially if the forest stand has 
no shrub layer or ground flora. If the vegetation is destroyed in total by

clear cutting, nutrient losses, especially nitrogen losses, may occur by in­
creased mineralization of soil organic matter and the cessation of plant up­
take. The nitrogen losses may reach 500 kg Ν ha"
 1 , in extreme cases even 
1000 kg Ν ha"
1, if, as a consequence of clear cutting and soil cultivation, 
the mor layer on top of the mineral soil is decomposed completely. On an 
annual basis this corresponds to average nitrogen losses of 0—5 (—10) kg 
Ν ha"
1 y "
1 in a coniferous forest region, assuming that the stands have a 
mean life span of 100 years and a balanced age distribution. 
6.4.5. Measurement and calculation of nutrient 
fluxes 
To describe the nutrient fluxes in a forest, Fig. 3 is acceptable only as a 
summary; for calculating the actual fluxes a more refined model is required. 
The following fluxes can be measured in the field by measuring the flow rate 
of the transport medium and its bioelement concentration. (Comparable 
fluxes in Fig. 3 are indicated with their usual numbers): 
(a) Input by rain (compare 31) 
(b) Input by interception by the canopy and bark (see Mayer and Ulrich, 
1974) (compare 31) 
(c) Total input = (a) + (b) (compare 31) 
(d) Transport by rain inside stand (sum of canopy drip and stem flow) 
(no comparable flux, may be included in 26) 
(e) Flow by litter fall (compare 26) 
(f) Growth increment (does not exist in Fig. 3) 
(g) Leaching from soil (water collection by suction lysimeter plates or 
candles) (compare 21) 
(h) Leaf leaching and (j) uptake from the atmosphere = (d) - (c) (compare 
15) 
(i) Uptake from soil = (e) + (f) + (h) (compare 30) 
(k) Total uptake = (i) + (j) (compare 30+ 31) 
(1) Change in soil reserves = (c) - (f) - (g) (not applicable) 
Table 30 shows a flux balance of a beech (Fagus silvatica) forest which 
may be used to outline the measurement and calculation of nutrient fluxes 
as described before. The ranges for nutrient flows shown in Table 31 for 
deciduous and coniferous forest stands with a regular age structure on acid 
soils of the northern hemisphere are taken from a set of IBP data (Ulrich et 
al., 1974).

--- Page 110 ---

107 
6.5. AGRO-ECOSYSTEMS IN F R A N C E (P. Jacquard) 
The nutrient cycling scheme as presented in Fig. 3 is in fact a very simpli­
fied one because the role of the microflora in the soil is not taken into ac­
count as a separate storage level for nutrients. We use schemes as presented in 
Figs. 4—12 to describe the cycling within the soil system. These figures de­
scribe how much of the fertilizer which was added at a certain time will be 
available for the next crop. This depends in particular on the fraction of 
pastures which is used for grazing. We have differentiated between the fol­
lowing systems: 
System A (French farm, all in pasture lands, without fertilizers); 
System Β (French farm, 75% of leys used by grazing); 
System C (French farm, 50% of leys used by grazing); 
System D (French farm, 25% of leys used by grazing); 
System Ε (French farm, no use of leys by grazing); 
System F (French farm, forage production without animals). 
The farms may be dairy and/or meat oriented, except, of course, in system 
F. The figures do not refer to areas in the farm, or to years in the same plot, 
but to the type of use of the forage production. 
Arable parts of the system were excluded; they were used only to test the 
availability of soil nutrients after leys. 
As regards the kind vegetation types, A is a mixed vegetation of perma­
nent grassland, Β may be a simple mixture (one grass + white glover), C (sys-

6.6. AGRO-ECOSYSTEMS IN C Z E C H O S L O V A K I A (J. Kolek) 
6.6.1. Introduction 
The organization and structure of agricultural enterprises in the socialist 
countries is characterized by substantial differences in the relationships of 
the land to property, as compared with the individual property. These 
differences, to a certain extent, result in differences in the nutrient flow. It 
can be noticed that: 
(a) The development of farm organization tends towards the formation of 
large associations of several agricultural enterprises, which together cultivate 
several thousand hectares of agricultural soil. 
(b) The associations (co-operative and state farms) create common enter­
prises for working up their primary products. 
(c) The most important change is that monogastric animals (pigs, poultry) 
are detached from the primary production in common enterprises so that, in 
the primary production, there remains only cattle and sheep breeding. 
(d) With this organization, agricultural production approaches a consider­
able way towards industrial production. 
6.6.2. Calculation method 
The annual balances of two co-operative farms served as a basis for the 
calculation of the fluxes. 
6.6.2.1. Plant production: the flow of nutrients 
For calculating the nutrient flow, we used, as bases, data concerning the 
purchase and application of industrial fertilizers to different crops, and the 
content of nutrients in precipitation (Hrbatf, 1974) and irrigation water 
(Antonovitf and Jacko, 1966). The content of nutrients in various parts of 
the plant (in the crop products and the plants' aerial parts) were calculated 
from Bajer's data (1973). The loss of nutrients by run-off was calculated 
after Övan&ra (1962), and losses by leaching after Bizik (1975). The Ν 
fixation was calculated according to Övaniara (1962), and volatilization 
according to Müller (1966). We did not obtain any data on mineralization, 
on reserves of soil nutrients or data on any enrichment of the soil from the 
geological substratum. 
For the uptake of nutrients from pastures, we started from the calcula­
tions of Krajiovii (personal communication, 1974). From the nutrient 
balances of both farms, we calculated then the quantity of nutrients which 
remained on the farm, the amount returned to the soil in the form of stall 
dung or post-harvest remains (Bajer, 1973) and how much had been export­
ed from the farms as primary products.

6.6.2.2. Animal production: 
the flow of nutrients 
For calculating nutrient flow from the plant component into the animal 
component, we calculated the nutrients contained in the forages of what­
ever origin, starting from the sub-system of plant production. The purchased 
feed mixtures were of standard composition and the contents of nutrients 
and energy were determined by the Czechoslovak Standard No. 467.007 
(Labuda, 1973). The content of NPK in animal bodies was calculated as 
follows. In horned cattle, we used the data of Schulz et al. (1974), and 
considered the average value of NPK contents in pieces weighing 480 kg 
(whole animals). For other animals, we did not obtain complete data on 
the NPK contents of their entire bodies and, therefore, we only used data 
on the commercially used parts of the animals (Strmiska, 1970). Thus 
the resulting values do not contain the NPK of skin, bones, entrails, etc., and 
so, to a certain extent, this distorts the final results (they would have been 
somewhat higher). The NPK content of milk was also calculated according 
to Strmiska (1970), and that of the sheep cheese according to Skopkovä et 
al. (1957). In the total balance of nutrients, we included solid and liquid 
excrements (Bajer, 1973), the loss of nitrogen from manure by volatilization 
and the nutrients lost in animals which died. 
6.6.2.3. The overall balance of nutrients 
By following the flow of NPK, it was impossible to calculate the effective­
ness of fertilizers in plant production, since we did not obtain data on the 
amounts of nutrients in the soil, on the mineralization of organic matter and 
on humification. Therefore, we were able merely to indicate the NPK 
amounts applied to the soil, the amounts exported by the crops and their 
flow through the animal component. 
6.6.3. The nutrient balance of two particular farms 
Classification. Intensive mixed system. Reference: Kolek-1; Slovakian low­
land farm, arable land, livestock without grass, use of supplemental feed, 
Table 46. 
6.6.3.1. Description of the JRD Teiedikovo 
The agricultural co-operative (JRD) of TeSedikovo covers 1853 ha, includ­
ing 1845 ha of arable soil. The number of workers in the co-operative in 
1973 was 498. The soil is a chernozem on loess. The mean annual precipi­
tation is about 56 cm, the mean annual temperature 9.2°C, and the altitude 
is 130 m. The structure of plant production is obvious from Table 44. In 
animal production, the structure was as follows: 882 homed cattle, 2630 
pigs, 362 sheep, 1950 ducks, 2410 geese and 2677 poultry. 
The JRD specializes mainly in the production of cereals. On the animal 
side, it is oriented towards milk production and rearing geese (in 1974, the 
number of the latter increased to 10 000).

--- Page 133 ---

130 
6.6.3.2. Production data 
Plant production. Wheat is the principal crop of this co-operative. It is culti­
vated on 550 ha with mean yields of 5451 kg ha~
1 . The entire wheat area 
received 106 041 kg N , 54 314 kg Ρ and 80 060 kg Κ from industrial fertiliz­
ers, precipitation and irrigation. In the form of solid grain, 51 670 kg Ν , 
10 330 kg Ρ and 12 310 kg Κ left the farm. The following amounts of nutri­
ents in grain (payments in kind, seeds, forage) remained on the farm: 8210 
kg N , 1636 kg Ρ and 1936 kg K. A further 7340 kg N, 1580 kg Ρ and 14 680 
kg Κ were removed in straw. From this quantity of straw, approximately 
one half was returned to the soil in the form of stable manure. Thus, per 100 
kg of grain, 1.6 kg N, 0.48 kg Ρ and 0.96 kg Κ were removed. 
In barley, the total nutrient content of grain was 11 850 kg N, 2667 kg Ρ 
and 3784 kg K, with a total nutrient input of 13 566 kg N, 12 356 kg Ρ 
and 16 917 kg K. The relatively high input of Ρ and Κ is surprising, in view 
of their export by the grain yield. Thus, for 100 kg grain, 2.07 kg N , 0.49 
kg Ρ and 0.8 kg Κ were removed. The total nutrient content of straw was 
1760 kg N, 210 kg Ρ and 1980 kg K, which was utilized as a whole in animal 
production (for horned cattle). 
Sugar beet was grown on 140 ha, and fertilized with 82 730 kg N, 31 261 
kg Ρ and 73 397 kg Κ (including the nutrients from stable dung, irrigation 
and rainfall). The industrial fertilizers used amounted to 19 600 kg N, 15 400 
kg Ρ and 28 000 kg K. The whole yield of 5 712 400 kg beet roots sold con­
tained 10 853 kg N, 2284 kg Ρ and 14 850 kg K. On the farm, there re­
mained beet-chips containing 9147 kg N, 1496 kg Ρ and 13 804 kg K; the 
chips were used for feeding horned cattle. For 100 kg of beet roots, the nut­
rient uptake was 0.2 kg N, 0.04 kg Ρ and 0.34 kg K. 
Grain maize was grown on 404 ha, providing a mean grain yield of 5024 
kg ha*
 1 . This area was fertilized with 78 600 kg N, 40 900 kg Ρ and 93 400 
kg K, including the following amounts of industrial fertilizers: 60 600 kg 
N, 36 400 kg P, and 80 800 kg K. For 100 kg grain, 2.5 kg N, 0.66 kg Ρ and 
2.2 kg Κ were removed. In the grain that was sold, nutrient removal from the 
farm amounted to 33 549 kg N, 8971 kg Ρ and 29 514 kg K. The members 
of the co-operative received cereals containing 9213 kg N, 1180 kg Ρ and 
3856 kg K. The maize remained on the field, thus returning 572 kg N, 110 
kg Ρ and 710 kg Κ to the soil. 
Silage maize, grown by the JRD on 101 ha with an average yield of 
21 654 kg ha~
 1 , is an important crop for the farm's animal production. It 
was fertilized with 16 200 kg N , 8100 kg Ρ and 18 200 kg K. The green bio­
mass harvested in the field contained 4190 kg N, 690 kg Ρ and 4840 kg K. 
The amounts removed per 100 kg dry matter were 1.9 kg N, 0.3 kg Ρ and 
2.2 kg K. The whole quantity of biomass produced was used for the produc­
tion of silage for horned cattle. 
The area for field vegetables was fertilized with 77 000 kg N , 28 900 kg 
Ρ and 67 100 kg K, of which industrial fertilizers amounted to 22 000 kg N,

--- Page 134 ---

131 
15 400 kg Ρ and 28 600 kg Κ. As for the uptake of nutrients, we could not 
assess the NPK content of irrigation water. The whole quantity of produced 
vegetables was sold; it contained 4539 kg N, 2960 kg Ρ and 3365 kg K. 
Perennial forages (clover and alfalfa) were grown on 420 ha, giving an 
average yield of 67 780 kg ha"
1 green biomass. One part of the forage area 
was fertilized with 46 200 kg N, 42 000 kg Ρ and 67 200 kg K, and the en­
tire area was irrigated. Part of the yield was utilized at the farm and had the 
following nutrient contents: 70 800 kg N, 10 120 kg Ρ and 62 080 kg K. 
Nutrients removed from the farm as sales in the form of alfalfa meal amount­
ed to 41 910 kg N, 8395 kg Ρ and 37 778 kg K. 
Annual forages were grown on 31 ha and were fertilized with the follow­
ing industrial fertilizers: 3100 kg N , 700 kg Ρ and 5000 kg K. The product 
remained on the farm for use in animal production and it contained 3150 kg 
N, 432 kg Ρ and 2646 k g K . 
Animal production. At the end of 1973, 889 horned cattle were reared on 
the farm, of which 408 were milk cows. The mean milking capacity was 
3112 litres yearly. At the beginning of the year, the live weight was 154 700 
kg and at the end 335 800 kg. 
The cattle did not graze, but were kept exclusively in stables. Since it was 
not possible to assess the changes in the herd during the year, and to distin­
guish the different age and productivity groups of animals, we simply con­
sidered the balance of nutrient flows based on the live weight of animals at 
the end of the year. 
The cattle were fed with bulk feed produced on the farm. During the 
whole year, the nutrient values amounted to 108 200 kg N, 19 400 kg Ρ 
and 103 900 kg K. When it comes to concentrates, it is the general practice 
for raw materials grown on the farm to be delivered to feedstuff manufactur­
ing plants and, in exchange, the JRD receives standard feeding mixtures. 
From these purchased concentrate feed mixtures, the horned cattle received 
18 970 kg N , 8400 kg Ρ and 16 880 kg K. From the feedstuff amounts men­
tioned, 153 200 kg of live weight was produced, and the losses by sale re­
present 162 500 kg of live weight (including the weight of cattle present on 
the farm in the previous year). Animal deaths resulted in loss of 6300 kg 
live weight. The NPK content in animal bodies was calculated according to 
Schulz et al. (1974), for one animal of 480 kg. The live weight (153 200 kg), 
produced from the feedstuffs of 1973 represents 3940 kg N, 960 kg Ρ and 
270 kg K. There was a removal from the farm of 162 500 kg live weight by 
sale, which represents 4420 kg N , 1016 kg Ρ and 303 kg K. Thus, the export 
from the farm is 4890 kg N, 1286 kg Ρ and 573 kg K. Altogether, 
1 270 000 1 of milk, containing 670 kg N, 270 kg Ρ and 220 kg Κ were 
produced and removed from the farm. Thus, the total nutrient export from 
the farm, in the sold cattle and milk, amounts to 4990 kg N, 1286 kg Ρ and 
573 kg K. The horned cattle yielded 13 460 kg N, 4940 kg Ρ and 20 540 kg 
Κ in solid and liquid excreta. Assuming that all the excreta go into the pro-

--- Page 135 ---

132 
auction of 14 000 tons of stable manure, the values mentioned return into 
the nutrient cycle of the farm, after, of course, subtracting the losses from 
volatilization of nitrogen. 
At the beginning of 1973, sheep rearing represented 800 kg in live weight 
of animals. At the end of the year, the live weight had increased to 7200 kg. 
Concentrate feedstuffs containing 80 kg N, 21 kg Ρ and 25 kg Κ were pur­
chased for the sheep. Bulk feeds were produced on the farm and represent 
3400 kg N, 612 kg Ρ and 2970 kg K. It is impossible to assess the consump­
tion from grazing because, in contrast to mountain farming, the lowland 
sheep graze harvest remains from the fields. From the above-mentioned live 
weight (7200 kg), 600 kg containing 10 kg N , 11 kg Ρ and 2 kg Κ (in meat) 
were sold. All the cheese produced on the farm, 88 600 kg, containing 22 
kg N, 9 kg Ρ and 4 kg K, was removed. It must be noticed that in the Te&e-
dikovo farm, sheep rearing is a supplementary component of animal produc­
tion. It is not possible to assess the amounts of nutrients returned to the soil 
as excrements. 
Poultry keeping (hens, geese, ducks) represents an important sector on the 
farm. At the beginning of 1973, no fowls were kept. By the end of this year, 
they had produced 151 100 kg of live weight containing 1143 kg N, 160 
kg Ρ and 180 kg K. At the same time, 99 000 eggs were sold, containing 
180 kg N, 36 kg Ρ and 21 kg K. For poultry keeping, standard concentrate 
feed mixtures were purchased, containing 1210 kg N , 3400 kg Ρ and 11 190 
kgK. 
Classification. Intensive mixed system. Reference: Kolek-2; Slovakian moun­
tain farm, mixed system, use of supplementary feed, Table 47. 
6.6.3.3. Description of the JRD Brusno 
JRD Brusno lies in mountainous country, at an altitude of 400—900 m. 
The soils are brown, acid, and the annual precipitation is 78 cm. The total 
area is 1281 ha, including 172 ha of arable land, 410 ha meadows and 699 ha 
pastures. In 1974, 188 persons worked in the JRD. 
The animal production had the following structure: 688 horned cattle, 
including 110 milk cows, and 557 sheep (the average annual cheese produc­
tion per ewe was 26.5 kg). The co-operative specializes in the production of 
milk, sheep cheese and wool. 
In contrast to the previous farm, JRD Brusno is principally oriented to 
the rearing of horned cattle and sheep. The topography and the relatively 
great dispersion of lands, lying mostly in hilly terrains, result in rather high 
costs for transport and for soil cultivation. 
6.6.3.4. Production data 
Plant production. Wheat was grown in this farm on 26 ha, giving an average 
yield of 3640 kg ha"
1. This area received the following amounts of nutrients

--- Page 136 ---

133 
(in kg, the figures in parentheses are the amounts of industrial fertilizers): 
3277 (2600) N , 2114 (2060) Ρ and 2699 (2600) K. The difference between 
the two values gives the supply of nutrients from precipitation and purchas­
ed seeds. A part of this area, containing 1070 kg N, 211 kg Ρ and 387 kg K, 
was allotted to the co-operative's members. In the form of purchased grain, 
980 kg N, 193 kg Ρ and 354 kg Κ were removed from the farm. Straw con­
tained 356 kg N, 40 kg Ρ and 406 kg Κ; it was used to produce stable 
manure. The uptake of nutrients, by the grain removed, was as follows (per 
100 kg of grain): 2.5 kg N, 0.48 kg Ρ and 1.14 kg K. The balance of NPK 
in this farm is shown in Table 45. 
Potatoes are the typical crop of mountainous regions. The co-operative 
grows them on 18 ha, with an average yield of 23 110 kg ha"
 1 . This area 
received nutrients from stable manure: 5800 kg N, 2200 kg Ρ and 7100 kg 
K, and from industrial fertilizers: 1800 kg N, 1580 kg Ρ and 3440 kg K. 
Thus, altogether, the following amounts of nutrients entered the soil (inclu­
sive of precipitation and seeds): 8105 kg N, 3857 kg Ρ and 10 862 kg K. A 
part of the yield was used as a feedstuff; it contained 322 kg N, 120 kg Ρ 
and 500 kg K. Removal as sold tubers accounted for 650 kg N, 260 kg Ρ 
and 940 kg K. The potato foliage left on the field contained 1296 kg N, 260 
kg Ρ and 1684 kg K. The utilization of nutrients per 100 kg product was 
0.3 kg N, 0.1 kg Ρ and 0.45 kg K. 
Barley was grown on only 21 ha, with an average yield of 3750 kg ha" \ 
To produce this yield, the whole area received 2634 kg N, 970 kg Ρ and 
2188 kg K, of which industrial fertilizers accounted for 2100 kg N, 930 kg 
Ρ and 2100 kg K. Part of the grain, containing 100 kg N, 24 kg Ρ and 30 kg 
K, was assigned to the co-operative's members. A further 1320 kg N, 316 kg 
Ρ and 396 kg Κ were removed as sold grain. The straw, containing 206 kg N , 
33 kg Ρ and 308 kg K, was used in animal production. The utilization of 
nutrients per 100 kg barley grain was 2 kg N, 0.45 kg Ρ and 0.9 kg K. 
Forages for silage, hay production and green fodder were the farm's main 
crops. In view of the various kinds of foods for different uses, we converted 
the yields from the total area, covering 1213 ha, into hay production. Then 
the total production of hay was 3 200 000 kg. To obtain this, the following 
nutrients from stable and liquid manures (to the annual silage crops) were 
used: 20 260 kg N, 4830 kg Ρ and 20 050 kg K. The industrial fertilizers, 
supplied to the annual forage and clover crops, amounted to 42 070 kg N , 
8740 kg Ρ and 52 320 kg K. After adding the nutrients from precipitation, 
the total entry of nutrients into the area under fodder plants increases to 
88 620 kg N, 14 794 kg Ρ and 76 011 kg K. The mass of feedstuffs produced 
contained 54 510 kg N, 9790 kg Ρ and 37 500 kg K. The whole quantity of 
fodder remained on the farm for rearing cattle and sheep beyond the grazing 
period (about 160 days of the year). 
Animal production. The JRD Brusno reared 578 horned cattle, including 
100 cows having a mean annual milking capacity of 3331 1. The purchased

fodders were standard mixtures containing, on the whole, the following 
amounts of nutrients: 7540 kg N, 1570 kg Ρ and 2050 kg K. The farm-grown 
bulk feed used for cattle rearing contained 47 280 kg N, 8510 kg Ρ and

duced, containing 366 kg N , 840 kg Ρ and 250 kg K. Loss from deaths 
amounted to 5800 kg live weight, containing 140 kg N, 34 kg Ρ and 10 kg 
K. Milk production was 323 300 kg, containing 170 kg N , 60 kg Ρ and 36 
kg K. All of the milk was sold. 
For 160 days of the year 470 horned cattle were put out to graze; they 
produced and left on the pasture 12 800 kg N, 5190 kg Ρ and 20 840 kg Κ 
in excrements. From this quantity 3800 kg is assumed to be lost by nitrogen 
volatilization. During stabling, the cattle produced 8780 kg N , 2750 kg Ρ 
and 8090 kg K, which went into the production of stable manure. 
Pigs reared in the co-operative had a total live weight of 16 400 kg, con­
taining 330 kg N , 30 kg Ρ and 48 kg Κ. From this quantity 7600 kg were 
sold, i.e. 152 kg N, 11 kg Ρ and 20 kg K, and the farm's own consumption 
was 8800 kg, containing 180 kg N , 19 kg Ρ and 28 kg K. The pigs produced 
excreta containing 590 kg N, 230 kg Ρ and 840 kg K. The live weight of 
pigs, produced on the farm, required the purchase of standard concentrate 
fodders containing 2303 kg N , 460 kg Ρ and 548 kg K.
