Productivity and nutrient balance: The total dry matter production is 7558 
kg ha"
1 y"
1, of which the above-ground part equals 3613 kg. With soil improve­
ment and manuring the above-ground part rises to 7650 kg ha"
1 y"
1. 
6.11.3.4. Shrub and tree savanna, Brazil 
The whole area is used for mixed farming. A distinction is made between 
areas receiving fertilizers and soil improvement, and areas without fertilizers 
and soil improvement. Both these areas are again split into a livestock part and 
an arable part. Thus in total, 6 systems have been defined. 
Classification: 
Mixed farming, Reference: Husz-4; Shrub and tree savannas, Central Brazil, 
no use of fertilizers or soil improvement, 25% cropped land and 75% grazed 
area, Table 90. 
Mixed, arable part. Reference: Husz-5; Shrub and tree savannas, Central 
Brazil, arable part of system Husz-4, Table 91. 
Mixed, livestock part. Reference: Husz-6; Shrub and tree savannas, Central 
Brazil, livestock part of system Husz-4, Table 92. 
Intensive mixed farming. Reference: Husz-7; Shrub and tree savannas, 
Central Brazil, with soil improvement and fertilizers, 50% cropped land, 50% 
livestock area, Table 93. 
Intensive, mixed, arable part. Reference: Husz-8; Shrub and tree savannas, 
Central Brazil, arable part of system Husz-7, Table 94. 
Intensive, mixed, livestock part. Reference: Husz-9; Shrub and tree savannas, 
Central Brazil, livestock part of system Husz-7, Table 95. 
Location: Central Brazilian plateau, mid-Brazil, Altitude 200—600 m. 
Climate: Mean annual temperature: 24—27°C; average temperature range: 
5°C; annual precipitation: 110—220 cm; mean precipitation: 127.2 cm; 
potential evapotranspiration: 330.2 cm; actual evapotranspiration: 24.9 cm. 
Soil: Although the landscape is geologically older, the tertiary erosion pro­
ducts and sediments (frequently sandstone from the upper Cretaceous) pre­
dominate at the soil surface. The tertiary sediments are extremely weathered. 
The soils on this material are Acric Ferrasols and they belong to the oldest 
known soil formations. The soils formed from the sandstone of the Cretaceous 
sediments are developed as Lithosols or ferralic Arenosols. During the Quarter-
nary period, the old landscape was altered once again. The soils resulting 
from sediments which were rearranged in the youngest geological era would 
appear to be Cambic or Argillic B-horizons. Main soils: Orthic Acrisols to­
gether with Ferric Luvisols, Eutric Nitrosols, Chromic Luvisols and Dystric 
Cambisols. Productivity index Qs = 0.50. 
Vegetation: Savannas of the "Campos Cerrados" (characteristic sorts of 
trees and shrubs) and gallery woods. 
Use: From a climatic point of view the production potential is high. How­
ever, the precipitation of 1200 mm annually and its distribution limit produc-

--- Page 261 ---

258 
tion. The mean shoot index is 0.478. The area is covered with savannas and 
gale forests; agriculture is only possible on selected areas, where the produc­
tion of tropical and subtropical crops such as coffee, citrus, cotton and sugar 
cane is possible. Intensive fertilizer dressings are used on the plantations, but 
on the areas belonging to small sociological units (families) fertilization prac­
tices vary enormously. The main products of the small farms are fruit, corn, 
beans, potatoes, and sweet potatoes. Livestock consists of horned cattle, pigs, 
horses and poultry. 
Productivity and nutrient balance: Nutrient balances have been prepared 
for the small farms; irrigated areas and plantations are not considered. No 
data for nutrient balances for separate farms are available, so that values again 
had to be calculated. For the system described by Husz-4 (no fertilizers, no 
soil improvement), Q s was taken to be 0.50, giving an above-ground dry mat­
ter production of 8266 kg ha"
1 . For the system Husz-5 (with fertilizers and 
soil improvement), Q s was set at 0.85, giving an above-ground dry matter 
production of 14052 kg ha"
1. To calculate the figures in the nutrient bal­
ance tables, the nutrient content of the crops was taken as: 10 kg N, 1.2 kg Ρ 
and 12 kg Κ per 1000 kg dry matter. The percentage of plant parts remain­
ing in the field was set at 20%, a further 57% went to the market, and the 
share of animal and human consumption was 22% and 1%, respectively. The 
actual figures for nitrogen are presented in Table 89, and the mutual rela­
tionships between the fluxes are shown in Fig. 41. 
An analysis of the nitrogen fluxes of system Husz-4, Tables 90—92, shows 
a shortage of nitrogen on the livestock area. This results from the assumption 
that an important part of the excrements are transferred to the arable area. If 
this is not done, the shortage of nitrogen occurs on the arable part of the farm. 
In such situations it is customary to alternate the arable land and the pastures 
from time to time. 
System Husz-5, Table 91 (see also Tables 89, 93, 94 and 95), shows a flow 
of nitrogen, in the form of feed for cattle, from the arable part to the pas­
tures. The return flow (application of manure to arable land) is absent, 
resulting in a nitrogen deficit for the arable part of the farm, in spite of the

fact that the fertilizers are applied to the arable area and not to the live­

Literature: Jenny, 1941,1964; Fried and Broeshart, 1967; Stevensen, 1965. 
6.11.3.5. Arid and mesophytic transitional woods 
Classification: Mixed livestock. Reference: Husz-10; Arid and mesophytic 
transitional woods, S. America, all kinds of products; ratio pastures : cropped 
land = 4:1, Table 96. 
Location: The arid woods and transitional forms to mesophytic woods are 
widely spread over this continent, on low plateaus and western slopes of the 
Andes (northern Chile, southern Peru), at altitudes of 500—1500 m. They 
also occur in the central Chaco from Santa Cruz de la Sierra, Bolivia, to Mar 
Chicita in Argentina (altitude 60—400 m), on the western edge of the Chaco 
(Argentina and Bolivia, altitude 100—500 m), in the eastern Chaco (altitude 
60—200 m), in the area of the Parana (Paraguay and Uruguay, altitude 20— 
100 m), in the area between the Rio Negro and Cordoba in mid-Argentina 
(0—400 m), in the Caatinga area (northwestern Brazil, 0—500 m) and in the 
Caribbean coast area, both in the valleys of the Andes with a semi-arid charac­
ter and the coast area from northern Peru to southern Ecuador. 
Climate: Mean annual temperature: 16—29°C; mean annual range: 14°C; 
annual precipitation: 30—150 cm; mean precipitation: 76.8 cm; mean poten­
tial evapotranspiration: 187.8 cm; actual evapotranspiration: 75.8 cm. 
Soil: Most sediments of these plateaus are weathered parts of an old moun­
tainous area. They form the present-day soil, although the original material 
(mainly of a sandy nature), which is usually displaced, has been changed a 
great deal due to tectonic movements and weathering. On the western slopes 
of the Andes Orthic Ferralsols, Xanthic soils, Albic Arenosols and Plinthic 
Acrisols are found (altitude: 60—400 m, temperature: 16—21°C ± 15, annual 
precipitation: 50—80 cm). Apart from the frequently occurring transition 
forms, the soils are mainly Acric Ferralsols, Orthic Acrisols and Ferralic 
Arenosols over the main part of the area, and, in the valley plains, both Ferric 
Luvisols and Gleysols within the reach of groundwater. The well known 
"Caatinga-lat sol" can be equated to an Orthic Ferralsol. In eroded areas, 
where ancient soils are removed, Rhodic Ferralsols and Eutric Nitrosols 
(= "Terra Roxa Estructurada") can be found, occurring on older geological 
material on solid rock (basalt). 
Vegetation: To describe the vegetation types, it is necessary to specify 
areas which can be distinguished. 
(a) South westwards from the Andes in northern Chile and in Peru. 
Altitude: 500—1500 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 16—21°C ± 7; precipitation 30—100 cm. 
Main vegetation: open arid brushwood with "pillar" cacti. 
Use: firewood; with irrigation: sugar-cane, cotton, rice, lucerne, maize.

--- Page 270 ---

267 
(b) Arid woods of the Central Chaco. (The Central Chaco runs from Santa 
Cruz de la Sierra, Bolivia, to the Mar Chiquita, Argentina.) 
Altitude: 100-400 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 20—24°C; mean annual range, 15°C; 
annual precipitation, 50—80 cm. 
Main vegetation: "Algarrobowood". 
Use: forestry, charcoal ("Fannin"), cattle, goats, sheep, cotton, maize, 
lucerne. 
(c) Arid woods to rather fresh woods of the eastern part of the Chäco (eastern 
Chaco in Paraquay and Argentina). 
Altitude: 60-200 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 24—25°C; mean annual range, 6°C; 
annual precipitation, 110—120 cm. 
Main vegetation: Quebracho-wood, Schinopsis balansae (Quebracho Colorado 
d i a q u e n o ) , Schinus molle (molle, pimiento), Copernicia australis ( c a r a n d a y ) , 
plus vegetation as in the Central Chaco. 
Use: as in the Central Chaco. 
(d) Mesophytic transitional woods of the western Chacorandes (western edge 
of the Chaco in Argentina and Bolivia). 
Altitude: 100-150 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 19—20°C; mean annual range, 13°C; 
annual precipitation, 70—100 cm. 
Main vegetation: Tala-Mistol wood. 
Use: forestry, sugar-cane, maize, lucerne, potatoes, tobacco, citrus, vegeta­
bles. 
(e) Park landscape of Entre Rios (Parque mesopotamico) (eastern Chaco 
border area in reach of the Parana, Paraguay and upper Uruguay). 
Altitude: 20-100 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 17—21°C; mean annual range, 12°C; 
annual precipitation, 100—140 cm. 
Main vegetation: subtropical woods, gallery woods, semi-arid and arid 
woods, palm woods, savannas within reach of the groundwater, rush-moors. 
Use: forestry, cattle, sheep, rice, cotton, jute, tobacco, fruit, wheat, maize, 
oats, lucerne, ground-nuts, sunflowers. 
(f) Arid woods of the Espinales and transition from arid woods to Monte 
vegetation (mid-Argentina between Rio Negro and Cordoba). 
Altitude: 0-400 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 14—18°C; mean annual range, 14°C; 
annual precipitation, 30—60 cm. 
Main vegetation: arid woods, palm woods, Monte shrub-steppe. 
Use: firewood, cattle, horses, lucerne. 
(g) Vegetation of arid valleys of the Andes (valleys of the Andes from north­
ern Argentina to Venezuela). 
Altitude: 400-2000 m.

--- Page 271 ---

268 
Climate: mean annual temperature depending on latitude and height, 
between 16 and 27°C; annual precipitation, 30—80 cm. 
Main vegetation: in Argentinian and Bolivian part: algarrobo woods and 
halophyte vegetation; in Peruvian, Ecuadorian, Columbian and Venezuelian 
part: arid woods. 
Use: firewood, cattle, goats, sheep, maize, wheat, rice, cotton, cocoa, fruit, 
vineyards. 
(h) Caribbean arid woods, mainly thorn bushes and cactus bushes (Caribbean 
coastal area of Columbia, Venezuela and neighbouring islands). 
Altitude: 0-500 (1000) m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 24—29°C; mean annual range, 1—2°C; 
annual precipitation, 30—80 cm. 
Main vegetation: thorn bushes, cacti, brushwoods. 
Use: firewood, cattle, goats, maize, cotton. 
(i) Caatinga (northeastern Brazil). 
Altitude: 0—500 m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 24—26°C; mean annual range, 1°C; 
annual precipitation, 30—80 cm. 
Main vegetation: arid woods and arid bushes of the Caatinga, gallery woods, 
arid grassland, savannas. 
Use: forestry, palm culture, palm oil, cattle, goats, sheep, hogs, donkeys, 
mules. 
(j) Agreste woods (between Atlantic coastal mountains and Caatinga). 
Altitude: 0-500 (1000) m. 
Climate: mean annual temperature, 25°C, mean annual range, 1°C; mean 
annual precipitation, 30—80 cm. 
Main vegetation: Agreste arid woods, palms, 
(k) Espinal of the mouth of the Plata (lower Parana, with delta). 
Altitude: 0—100 m. 
Climate: mean annual precipitation, 17°C; mean annual range, 12°C; 
annual precipitation, 80—100 cm. 
Main vegetation: thorn brushwoods, willow woods, tessaria woods, 
pithecolobium woods, saphium woods, erythrina woods, palm woods. 
Use: fruit culture. 
Productivity and nutrient balance: In this whole area forestry is important: 
the nutrient balance applies however to the (extensive) mixed farming which 
is carried out near villages. The ratio between pastures and cropped land can 
be taken as 4:1. The calculations are based on a mean yield of 5945 kg ha
- 1 
above-ground dry matter, and a soil productivity index Qs of 0.605. The total 
dry matter production is 14 940 kg ha"
1 without soil improvement (shoot 
index Q v = 0.398).

6.11.3.6. Arable farming of a tropical and sub-tropical nature, fruit and 
horticulture included 
This system can be divided into two subsystems. 
— Plantation system on 80% of the total agricultural surface 
— Small farm system on 20% of the total agricultural surface. 
Classification: 
Intensive arable. Reference: Husz-11; Tropical and subtropical arable farm­
ing, small farm system, Table 97. 
Intensive arable. Reference: Husz-12; Tropical and subtropical arable farm­
ing, plantation system, Table 98. 
Location: All over the continent, as far as ecological and topographical 
conditions and the traffic situation make it possible. The total surface in­
volved is small (about 2%). It is distributed, with few exceptions along the 
coast of south and east Brazil, the Caribbean coasts and river oases in the arid 
zone of the Pacific coast. 
Climate: The climate is heterogeneous within the framework of tropical 
conditions and varies from a humid-warm equatorial to a sub-tropical desert 
climate (with irrigation). However, high temperatures of more than 25°C al­
ways last for at least 6 months, and mean temperatures do not fall below 
14°C. Most of the time, rainfall periods and dry periods can be clearly distin­
guished. Mean precipitation, 156.3 cm; mean potential evapotranspiration, 
203.4 cm; mean actual evapotranspiration, 146.9 cm. 
Soil: As a result of the widespread occurrence of this ecosystem, the soils 
are also very heterogeneous. However, in general, there are fertile well situat­
ed soils, under a mixture of arable farming and plantation culture. These soils 
are mainly on younger deposits of terraced landscapes (Riverside soils or 
colluvial soils) and flat-conical deposits from river systems: Eutric Fluvisols, 
Eutric Regosols, Ochric, Humic or Calcic Gleysols, Saline Vertisols, Eutric 
Planasols, Phaeozems. 
The oldest geological formations in southeast Brazil consist of acid crystal­
lines (granite, gneiss, quartzite) encysted by red sandstone. They form acid, 
less fertile red soils (Ferric Luvisols, Humic Cambisols, or Acrisols). However, 
under the influence of spurs of the central Brazilian mountains (basic crystal­
line), weakly acid to neutral soils could be developed (Rhodic Ferrasols, 
Humic Ferrasols). In the neighbourhood of the coast, marine deposits also 
form soil (Xanthic Ferrasols and Orthic Acrisols). Mean productivity index, 
Q s , 0.512 (0.9 on irrigated soils). 
Use: The plantations cover about 80% of the agricultural area, the small 
farms occupy about 20%; livestock is almost absent. 
Productivity and nutrient balance: The plantations are very well fertilized, 
but the small farms, noticing the high yields on the plantations, also use more 
fertilizers than usual on such small farms in South America. The mean shoot 
index is 0.453 (0.56 on irrigated soils). The mean yield is 11 360 kg ha"
1 
above-ground dry matter, and with irrigation 19 960 kg ha"
1. For optimal

conditions (soil improvement and irrigation) a yield of 33 600 kg ha"
1 seems 
possible. The main products are maize, rice, potatoes and sweet potatoes, 
sugar-cane, bananas, pineapple, coffee, cacao, pulses, tropical and sub-tropical 
fruits (mango, cherimoya, avocado) (Husz, 1968). 
6.11.3.7. Arable farming in a temperate climate, with green fodder and 
meadows (intensive grasslands and dairy) 
Classification: Intensive mixed. Reference: Husz-13; Mixed system, temperate 
zone, S. America, 33% arable farming, 67% livestock farming, Table 99. 
Location: Eastern part of mid-Argentina from Rio Uruguay to Bahia Bianca 
(plain, pampa) and Uruguay and south Brazil (hill, pampa): from the east side 
of the Rio Uruguay to the Atlantic coast. 
Climate: Mean annual temperature: 14—19°C; mean annual range: 12°C; 
annual precipitation: 60—130 cm; mean precipitation: 93.4 cm; mean poten­
tial evapotranspiration: 148.9 cm; mean actual evapotranspiration: 91.9 cm. 
Soil: The pampas extend over about 500 000 km
2 and are mainly covered 
with quaternary deposits. They represent the present land surface and are 
soil-forming. The soils are loess-like silty loamy soils. Loam and clay loam 
occur only in basins or on river beds. The deposits are calcareous and rich in 
plagioclas, hornblende, pyroxenes and volcanic glass (influence of volcanic 
ash from the youngest geological era). The rather humid zone of the hill pam­
pas in the north contains fine granular material. Phaeozems with an argillic Β 
horizon have been formed, and when the texture is very fine, vertisols can be 
found. The argillic Β horizon is absent in the western part, as it is more arid 
here and the texture of the basic material is coarser, so that sand dunes are some­
times formed. The Phaeozems without a Β horizon can be found in combination 
with Rhegosols. In the lowlands mainly saltic gleys and pseudogleys occur (gleyic 
solontschaks). The salinization is more intensive in the eastern pampa, where 
the natural drainage of the soil is bad and where lakes have formed in flat 
places. In this area, Mollic Solonetz, Mollic Planosols and Mollic Gleysols can 
be found. In the south-east, soils with a hardened CaC03-horizon are predom­
inant, and in this area, where deposits (mostly of aeolic origin) slightly cover 
this CaC03-horizon, Phaeozems and Planosols have been formed; in the basins 
Solonetza. In eroded places, the CaC03-horizon lies at the soil surface and is 
soil-forming; then rendzinas are developed. Soil quality index, Qs = 0.742. 
Use: With the exception of the plantations on the tropical and sub-tropical 
areas, these systems are the most intensively used ones. The yields are low 
and can be compared to those of Europe in the thirties. Pastures and arable 
land alternate as a result of a special renting system which requires lucerne to 
be sown at the end of a renting period of a few years. It can then be used as a 
pasture for 5—10 years, practically without any further application of fertiliz­
ers. Thereafter, the soil is ploughed again; maize, wheat, sunflowers and linseed 
are the main crops.

--- Page 279 ---

276 
Productivity and nutrient balance: The climatic conditions favour symbiotic 
nitrogen fixation. For lucerne, it is estimated that from 50 to 650 kg of 
nitrogen ha"
1 can be fixed annually. If 150 kg Ν ha"
1 is taken as a mean and 
it is assumed that lucerne covers 25% of the pastures, the mean nitrogen fixa­
tion on grassland is 37.5 kg Ν ha"
1. With 2/3 pastures and 1/3 arable land, the 
mean nitrogen fixation is 25 kg ha"
1. The systems appear to be in equilibrium 
with applications of 45 kg of fertilizer Ν ha"
1 arable land (15 kg Ν ha"
1 agri­
cultural area). The shoot index Q v = 0.444. This is 10 100 kg shoot dry matter 
ha"
1, or 12 250 kg ha"
1 with soil improvement (FAO and UNESCO, 1967; 
Duckham and Masefield, 1970; Fried and Broeshart, 1967).

--- Page 280 ---

277 
Chapter 7 
7.1. I N T R O D U C T I O N 
At the start of the symposium Dr Geertsema expressed the hope that the 
discussions would shed more light on the possibilities of increased food pro­
duction for human society without wastage of resources and energy, and 
without undue damage to the environment. It was also hoped that areas of 
well defined knowledge, matters of divided opinion, and needs for future 
research might be identified. 
In this chapter an attempt is made to assess the contribution of the sym­
posium towards these problems. In an experimental excercise of this kind 
generalizations are inevitable and any tentative conclusions reached here 
should be carefully considered for specific systems, and reconsidered in the 
light of subsequent new knowledge. At the symposium, Kolenbrander pre­
sented an analysis of the relationship between farm inputs and product 
(consumable) output which is an assessment of production efficiency. A 
modified approach is discussed in the section which follows, on nutrient 
balances, by Frissel and Kolenbrander, together with their conclusions re­
garding losses of nutrients. Floate, in the section on changes in pool sizes, 
has attempted to identify common features among the set of systems, and to 
analyse, for those systems losing nutrients from the total soil pool, the 
source of that loss from the subsidiary pools within the soil. From this analy­
sis he has attempted to draw conclusions regarding the state of soil organic 
matter, and the fate of applied fertilizers. 
The possible manipulation of systems through use of fertilizers is discus­
sed and the section concludes with a review of the needs for further research. 
7.2. THE N U T R I E N T B A L A N C E S ; S U M M A R I Z I N G GRAPHS A N D TABLES 
(Frissel and Kolenbrander) 
A summary of the inputs and consumable outputs is shown in Table 100. 
This table also contains a qualitative indication of the state of balance of the 
nutrients Ν, Ρ and K. The symbols +, 0 or - indicate whether the system 
gains, is in balance or loses the nutrient. 
Details are shown in Figs. 42—52 and Tables 101 and 102. Both inputs 
vary over wide ranges, which made it necessary to use logarithmic scales. In 
the figures which relate consumable outputs, losses to the atmosphere and 
leaching losses to the farm inputs, lines are drawn with equal outputs or 
equal losses, both being expressed as a percentage of the farm input. Figures 
which show the consumable outputs versus the farm inputs also contain 
lines (the dotted ones) with equal absolute losses (in kg nutrient ha"
 1 y~
 1 ) . 
In fact, all values refer to a period of one year; this is not indicated in order 
to avoid overloading the figures.

ducts such as grains, roots, tubers, leaves (in the case of vegetables and tea),

hay, milk and meat, but not straw and plant residues remaining on the field. 
For forests, wood which is carried off is considered. The farm input includes 
fertilizers, manure and inputs via irrigation and rain, but also the input via 
biological nitrogen fixation. Systems in which biological nitrogen fixation 
exceeds 35 kg ha"
 1 y~
 1 are indicated with closed symbols. Mineralization 
of the initial amount of soil organic matter is not considered as a farm input. 
Fig. 42 shows that the output of all arable systems is between 30 and 
100% of the input, the only exception with a considerably lower output 
being the system Newbould and Floate-6 (Meathop Wood) which appears to 
have a very high nitrogen fixing capacity (100 kg Ν ha~
 1 v~
 1 ). 
Up to a farm input of 150 kg Ν ha"
 1 y"
 1 , the output efficiencies are 
often close to the 66% efficiency curve. For inputs above 150 kg Ν ha"
 1 
y
_ 1 , the outputs are scattered around the 50% efficiency curve, indicating 
slightly lower efficiencies at higher farm inputs. 
Fig. 43 shows the same type of graph for the livestock systems. Scattering 
of data points is much wider than for arable systems. About half of the 
points are within the efficiency range of 10 to 30%. A few systems have a 
better efficiency (Husz-6, Jacquard-1 and -3). Eight systems have efficiencies

--- Page 285 ---

282 
between 3 and 10% and one system reaches an efficiency of no more than 
1%. This system, Damen-1, describes the livestock part of a Dutch farm 
in 1800. The low efficiency can be explained by the fact that the main task 
of that part of the farm was not food production, but production of manure 
which amounted to 35 kg Ν ha"
 1 y
 
1. 
The absolute nitrogen losses of livestock systems are much higher than 
those of arable systems. In three cases the losses are even higher than 500 
kg Ν ha"
 1 y~
 1 (Jacqaurd-6, Henkens-3 and -4). The surprising fact that these 
systems have efficiencies similar to systems with much lower inputs, can 
partly be explained by the fact that the input is mainly in the form of feed 
(158 and 369 kg Ν ha"
1 y"
 1 for systems Henkens-3 and-4, respectively). 
Fig. 44 shows the results of the mixed systems. As could be expected, the 
results are intermediate between those of arable and livestock systems; ex­
treme values are missing. A remarkable exception is system Husz-4 with an 
efficiency of 200% (input 11 kg Ν ha"
1 y"

1 ) . 
Underestimation of the biological nitrogen fixation (estimated at 5 kg Ν 
ha
- 1 y "

Nitrogen leaching data are shown in Fig. 45. They range from 1 to 100% 
of the farm input and rise to 90 kg Ν ha"
 1 y"
 1 . Highest values are reported 
for the systems Thomas and Gilliam-5, Kolek-1 and Yatazawa-3 and -6 (pro­
ducts: cotton, mixed farming products, vegetables and tea, respectively). 
From a few systems, reported leaching losses are below 1 kg Ν ha
- 1 y
- 1. For 
some other systems no leaching data are given. These are all grouped near the 
abscissa of Fig. 45 and indicated with < 1 . A careful study of the original da­
ta, on which Fig. 45 is based, shows that the leaching data of Jacquard (sym­
bol ^ ) are rather rough estimates and probably are less reliable than the oth­
er leaching data for farm inputs below 200 kg Ν ha~
 1 y~
 1 . The data of the 
systems Henkens-3 and -4 (symbols ν on the extreme right-hand side of Fig. 
45) are difficult to compare with the other data because the share of import­
ed feed proteins for cattle is so high. If the mentioned values are disregarded, 
it appears that leaching losses for farm inputs below 150 kg Ν ha
- 1 y"
1 are 
scattered around the 10% leaching curve, while for inputs above 150 kg Ν 
ha"
 1 y~
1 , they are scattered around the 20% leaching curve. Biological 
nitrogen fixation does not seem to influence the leaching percentage. 
Although for a particular site the amount leached per ha is, from a pollu­
tion point of view, decisive, this need not be true if the food production is 
considered on a world-wide scale. On the latter basis, the ratio kg Ν leached: 
kg Ν in consumable output is more important. These ratios are listed in Fig.

Nitrogen losses to the atmosphere are shown in Table 101 and Fig. 47. 
Table 101 shows the losses by volatilization of ammonia as well as those by 
denitrification. It should be noted that fewer data are available for this 
source of loss than for leaching and even when figures are available they are 
often best estimates only. Fig. 47 presents the combined effect of both pro­
cesses. Open symbols indicate that volatilization of ammonia dominates, and 
closed symbols that denitrification dominates. For a number of systems no 
or very low losses to the atmosphere were reported. These systems are 
grouped near the abscissa and indicated with < 1 . For most of the livestock 
systems, losses are between 20 and 66% and volatilization of ammonia domi­
nates. For arable systems most losses are between 3 and 30% and denitrifica­
tion dominates. The highest loss reported for volatilization of ammonia 
equals 98 kg Ν ha"
1 y "
1 (system Thomas + Gilliam-6), the highest value for 
denitrification equals 192 kg Ν ha"

7.2.2. Phosphorus 
The output of consumable phosphate versus the farm input is shown in 
Fig. 49, and data provided in Table 100. It is remarkable that for farm inputs 
lower than 1 kg Ρ ha""
1, the output is often higher than the input (systems: 
Henkens-1, Dutch dairy farm; Ulrich-1, -2 and -3, all three forests; Thomas + 
Gilliam-7 and -8, both forests; Newbould + Floate-6, woodland). In all these 
systems no Ρ fertilizers are used. Most probably they indicate a steady miner­
alization from soil, supplying some 1—2 kg Ρ ha~
1 y~
1 . Because the outputs 
are measured more accurately than the input, it is, however, also possible 
that inaccuracies are involved. For the extensive systems described by Husz 
and Noy-Meir + Harpaz, often no input data are available. For the systems 
which use fertilizers, the output ranges from 10 to 100% of the input; for 
most of them the output is lower than 50%. The two systems with the high­
est phosphate output (25 and 31 kg Ρ h a
- 1) are Henkens-5 and -6, both

--- Page 292 ---

289 
Fig. 49. The output of consumable phosphate vs the farm input of phosphate. The shapes 
of the symbols indicate the authors: see also Fig. 42. The full lines connect points with 
equal efficiencies (100 X output/input). The dotted lines connect points with equal abso­
lute gains or losses (kg Ρ h a
- 1 y
_ 1 ). Systems for which the farm input is below 0.1 kg Ρ 
h a
- 1 y
- 1 are grouped near the ordinate and indicated with < 0.1. 
Dutch arable farms with crop rotations. The 100% efficiency which is reach­
ed here is caused by the large phosphate dressings of the last decades, so that 
the top layer of the soil no longer fixes and all phosphate is available for up­
take by the crop. The livestock system Henkens-4 (Dutch dairy farm, input 
87 kg Ρ ha
- 1, output 22 kg P) has a much lower efficiency. This is because 
the phosphate input is higher than required; this surprising phenomenon is 
caused by the fact that the phosphate supply takes place via supplemental 
feed and manure and not at all via fertilizers. The supplemental feed contains 
more phosphate than is required for the pastures of the farm. Many of the 
other low efficiencies must probably be attributed to phosphate fixation by 
the soils. For a few other systems the cause of the high efficiency is unclear. 
Phosphate leaching is below 1 kg Ρ ha"
1 in all systems, and below 0.1 kg Ρ 
ha~
 1 in almost all systems. The highest leaching losses reported are: 
Yatazawa-1, 0.9 kg Ρ ha"
 1 ; Newbould + Floate-9, 0.5 kg ha~
1 ; Newbould + 
Floate-1, -2 and -3, 0.4 kg ha~
 1 . There is no discernible relation between 
phosphate leaching and phosphate fertilization. Losses due to run-off are 
only reported for a few systems. For the systems Thomas + Gilliam-1, -2,

--- Page 293 ---

290 
-3, -4 and -5 run-off losses are respectively 3, 3, 3, 5 and 1 kg Ρ ha"
 1 , and 
so considerably higher than the leaching values. 
Fig. 50 shows the N:P ratios of the consumable output. In general the 
ratios are in agreement with common values (grain 4.9, potatoes 5.0, beets 
4.1—4.2, hay 7.7, clover 9.7, fodder maize 5.3, sugar beet tops 8.3, fodder 
beet tops 11.4, milk 6.3, animals (as a whole) 4.3, — Kolenbrander, personal 
communication). A few ratios deviate substantially, especially those of the 
systems Husz-1 and -2 (symbols · in lower left corner of Fig. 50). In his sys­

systems are extremely phosphate deficient.

7.3. C H A N G E S IN SOIL POOLS (Floate) 
It has been suggested that some generalizations about changes in the 
amounts of nutrients in the organic, mineral and available soil pools, brought 
about by agricultural practice may be possible by an analysis of data pre­
sented at the symposium. 
Very few data were presented on the amounts of nutrients entering and 
leaving the soil mineral pool, probably because there is little reliable infor­
mation available on rates of weathering from soil minerals, and the propor­
tions of applied fertilizers removed from soil by fixation. Table 103 sum­
marizes the data presented to the symposium. These data are insufficient 
for general conclusions to be drawn but they suggest some loss of Κ from 
soil minerals in the absence of added fertilizer, and fixation of applied Ρ 
fertilizer by the soils of certain agro-ecosystems. 
The data for changes in the soil organic pool are summarized in Table 104, 
which shows that of 38 systems for which data are available, only three are 
losing any major nutrient and 26 are increasing in one or more of those 
nutrients*. The only farming systems losing nutrients from the organic pool

from the sum of the number of systems in each category listed in Table 104 and the 
following tables. This arises because fertilizers do not always include all three major 
nutrients, and because systems or pools may be gaining one nutrient while simultaneously 
losing another.

The data also show that without added fertilizers more than half of the

the pool. It is therefore not possible to state from the data available to this 
symposium the length of time for which nutrients can be supplied to crops 
without limiting growth. In any case it is probable that the losses are exag­
gerated by lack of good data on rates of mineralization from organic re­
serves, and on rates of release by weathering: both are probably underestima­
ted by many authors.

(see footnote to page 292). The data in Table 107 show the details of nutri-

that soil losses should not exceed gains. This analysis has drawn attention to 
the fact that many extensive, and some intensive systems appear to be in 
soil nutrient deficit. There are insufficient data to be able to conclude that 
the supply of nutrients for these systems will become critical, but there is a 
clear need for more reliable data for the less well known sources of nutrient 
supply and loss, and possibly a case for increasing fertilizer applications in 
some systems.

7.4. E X C H A N G E OF N U T R I E N T S BETWEEN ATMOSPHERE A N D V E G E T A T I O N -
SOIL (Rosswall) 
7.4.1. Introduction 
Exchanges of compounds between the vegetation—soil system and the 
atmosphere play a major role in the functioning of ecosystems, the major 
transport being that of carbon fixed by photosynthesis in the form of carbo­
hydrates and released by respiration as carbon dioxide. The regulation of the 
productivity of an ecosystem is thus to an important degree dependent on 
atmospheric parameters such as carbon dioxide concentration and light, but 
also the nutrient cycles are controlled by processes involving the atmosphere. 
The major part of nutrients is cycled in the vegetation—soil compartment 
of any terrestrial ecosystem; on a global scale the exhanges of, for example, 
nitrogen to and from the atmosphere amount to only 15% of the transfers of 
nitrogen between vegetation and soil (Rosswall, 1976). If the circulation of 
nitrogen through the soil micro-organisms is taken into account, only less 
than 5% of the annual nitrogen flow in the global terrestrial system passes 
through the atmosphere. 
Nitrogen is the only nutrient which occurs in the atmosphere in any 
appreciable amount, but in the form of molecular nitrogen, which is only 
available to certain bacteria and blue-green algae. Sulphur has been added to 
the atmosphere increasingly in recent years through combustion of fossil 
fuel in industrial regions, resulting in increased amounts in dry and wet 
deposition. It is probable that sulphur would have to be added as fertilizers 
to some agricultural areas, if the system started to receive smaller quantities 
of sulphur from the atmosphere as a result of e.g. new rules being brought 
into force concerning the emissions of sulphur dioxide through combustion. 
The importance of air and precipitation as sources of nutrients has been 
pointed out in several reviews (see for example Tamm, 1958, Eriksson, 
1966). There are, however, only minor transfers of nutrients other than 
nitrogen and sulphur between the atmosphere and the vegetation—soil, and 
e.g. phosphorus and potassium will not be considered in this section. It 
should be noted that additions of sea spray may add important amounts of 
certain nutrients in coastal regions. 
Nutrients are also lost to the atmosphere from the soil, but although 
sulphur can be lost in the form of various organic sulphides or as hydrogen 
sulphide under anaerobic conditions, the only important transfer of nutri­
ents to the atmosphere, apart from wind erosion, is the loss of nitrogen, as 
N 2 or N 2 O, through denitrification of nitrate by soil micro-organisms and 
as N H 3 through volatilization. 
7.4.2. Nutrient additions from the atmosphere 
The most important addition of nutrients to agro-ecosystems from the

--- Page 300 ---

297 
atmosphere is through symbiotic nitrogen fixation by Rhizobium bacteria 
in association with leguminous crops. In 1972, 123.7 Χ 10
6 ha of grain 
legumes in the world produced 113.6 Χ 10
6 tons of seed. Similarly, forage 
legumes are important and, excluding meadows and grasslands, 135 Χ 10
6 ha 
are cultivated with these crops in the world (Evans, 1975). Legumes can fix 
up to 500 kg Ν ha~
 1 y"
 1 (Vincent, 1974), and agricultural legumes have 
been calculated to be responsible for 25% of the global terrestrial biological 
nitrogen fixation (Burns and Hardy, 1975). 
Free-living, nitrogen-fixing micro-organisms can also be important in some 
agro-ecosystems; i.e. blue-green algae in rice crops (Venkataraman, 1975) 
and bacteria {Spirillum lipoferum) in the rhizosphere of C4-grasses in trop­
ical areas (Döbereiner, 1977). 
Generally the additions of nitrogen from the atmosphere through wet and 
dry deposition to agro-ecosystems are small with regard to the needs of the 
crop (Table 108). On a global scale, however, the amount of N H 3 /NH 4 -N 
added from the atmosphere through wet and dry deposition to the terrestrial 
system has been estimated at 6—12 kg Ν ha
- 1 as compared to an addition of 
9 kg ha~
 1 through biological nitrogen fixation (calculated from Söderlund 
and Svensson, 1976). The additions of nitrate-N and organic-N are smaller 
(Table 109). It can be seen that on the average fertilizers contribute only 
8—14% of the annual additions of nitrogen to the vegetation—soil complex. 
There are, however, large local and regional differences with regard to the 
use of nitrogen fertilizers, and this spatial variability also applies to many 
other parts of the nitrogen cycle; the data are presented only to put in pers­
pective the nonfertilizer additions of nitrogen to the terrestrial ecosystem 
from the atmosphere. 
As a regional example of nitrogen inputs from the atmosphere as com­
pared to man-made additions, Miller and Smith (1976) have made a nitrogen 
balance study for the Southern San Joaquin Valley in California. This is an 
intensively cultivated region with 50% of the total area under irrigation; 
cotton is grown on 26% of the area, barley on 19% and grapes on 12%. 
About 21% of the total nitrogen additions were directly from the atmos­
phere (Table 110) ^nd thus contributed a significant amount, but this was 
largely due to the growth of legumes (16.6%). 
There are also important local transfers of nitrogen from the atmosphere 
to the soil. Ammonia has a short turnover time in the atmosphere and a 
large part of the ammonia volatilized from the soil under vegetation will be 
readsorbed by the canopy before reaching the open air (Denmead et al., 
1976). Ammonia given off from grazed pastures (Denmead et al., 1974) 
and feedlots (Hutchinson and Viets, 1969) can efficiently be adsorbed by 
the soil and vegetation of local fields. 
Additions of nitrogen by wet and dry deposition can be important in 
some ecosystems and the productivity may depend on them, although they 
are numerically small. One example of this is the ombrotrophic tundra 
mire described by Rosswall et al. (1975). Tamm (1953), investigating the

7.4.3. Nutrient losses to the atmosphere 
Nitrogen is the only nutrient which can be lost to the atmosphere from 
the soil-^vegetation system in appreciable amounts, and this loss occurs as 
ammonia, nitrogen oxides and molecular nitrogen. The losses of nitrogen 
compounds to the atmosphere from the sites considered in this report are 
presented in Table 101. Most systems from which nitrogen is considered to 
be lost to the atmosphere have losses of 10—50% of the farm input. 
Ammonia is lost through volatilization, a process which is highly depen­
dent on the pH. Large amounts of ammonia are lost from manure (e.g. Lauer 
et al., 1976) and important losses can also occur after the addition of urea or 
ammonia fertilizers (e.g. Nömmik, 1967). Denmead et al. (1974) compared 
ammonia losses from grazed and nongrazed pastures and found that grazing 
increased ammonia losses. It should also be noted that gaseous nitrogen pro­
ducts escape from animals and nearly 12% of the nitrogen consumed has 
been estimated to be lost during digestion (Dean et al., 1975). 
With regard to the losses of molecular nitrogen and nitrogen oxides, these 
are brought about through biological (end products N 2 and N 2 O) and 
sometimes chemical (NO and N 0 2 ) processes. Biological denitrification, 
which reduces nitrate to gaseous end-products, is an anaerobic process and 
may be of great significance, for example in waterlogged soils or if nitrate 
concentrations are high at the time of heavy rainfall. Loss rates from nitro­
gen fertilizers have been reported to vary between 1 and 75% (Cast, 1976), 
but an average loss of nitrogen from fertilizers through denitrification seems 
to be 10-15% (Broadbent and Clark, 1965). The data in Table 101 show 
that in the present survey, 2—39% of the input of nitrogen to the system 
was lost through denitrification. As a percentage of the fertilizer input, the 
losses were even greater (2—73%, mean of 22%; Tables 100, 101). It should 
be added, however, that actual measurements of losses due to denitrification 
in the field are almost non-existent. 
Recent interest in the rates of denitrification of nitrogen added as fertiliz­
ers in agriculture is due to the possibilities of a large-scale production of 
nitrous oxide ( N 2 O). The release of N 2 Ο has important implications for the 
stratospheric ozone layer, as nitrous oxide ( N 2 O) is converted to nitric oxide

--- Page 304 ---

301 
(NO) which in turn, together with nitrogen dioxide ( N 0 2 ) , reacts with ozone 
( 0 3 ) resulting in a catalytic destruction of ozone according to the formula: 
0 (
1 D ) + N 2 0 - ^ 2 N O 
NO + 0 3 - > N 0 2 + 0 2 
0 3 + hi; -> Ο + 0 2 
N 0 2 + Ο - > NO + 0 2 
Net results: 2 0 3 -> 30 2 (Crutzen and Ehhalt, 1977). 
(0(* D) is atomic oxygen in an electronically excited state, which is produc­
ed from ozone and radiation; λ ^ 310 nm). 
It has been estimated that a global increase in nitrous oxide production 
by 20% will decrease the total ozone by 4% (Crutzen, 1974) and this, in 
turn, would result in an 8% increase in skin cancer (Grobecker et al., 1975). 
With the data available at present it is difficult to make a rigorous assessment 
of the magnitude of the impact on the ozone layer of nitrous oxide produc­
ed from nitrogen fertilizers. The problem is, however, receiving considerable 
attention, and WMÖ has recommended further studies on the nitrogen cycle 
(WMO, 1976). Only by a better understanding of all the facets of the global 
nitrogen cycle can the importance of N 2 Ο production from fertilizers be 
evaluated. 
N 2 Ο also contributes to the "greenhouse effect" by reducing the escape 
of thermal heat to the outer atmosphere, and it has been estimated that a 
doubling of the N 2 Ο concentration will result in an increase by 0.7° Κ in 
the global surface temperature (Wang et al., 1976). 
There is a lack of data on in situ rates of denitrification and on the pro­
portion of N 2 Ο vs N 2 in the end product from all types of soil. It can be 
hoped that recently published results on the inhibition of nitrous oxide 
reduction by acetylene (Balderston et al., 1976; Yoshinari and Knowles, 
1976) will lead to the development of new techniques for determining deni­
trification rates in natural environments. 
Sulphur is also lost from soil to atmosphere under anaerobic conditions 
through the reduction of sulphate to gaseous hydrogen sulphide. Bloomfield 
(1969) reported that 20—40% of the sulphate added to two topsoils was 
released as hydrogen sulphide. Sulphur gases are also evolved from animal 
manure, but the total amounts seem to be small (Banwart and Bremner, 
1975). 
On a global scale, the release of gaseous sulphur compounds is in the 
order of 7.5 kg S ha"
1 y"
 1 (Sirnan and Jansson, 1976a). Most of this does, 
however, originate from swamps and marshy areas and it seems as if decom­
posing terrestrial plant litter supplies only 0.1—0.3 kg S ha"
1 y~
1 (Granat 
et al., 1976). In addition, 0.1—0.3 kg S ha"
1 y"
 1 is lost in the form of or­
ganic sulphur compounds from soils (Hitchcock, 1975). The release of or-

--- Page 305 ---

302 
ganic volatile sulphur compounds from agricultural soils seems to be very 
small (Banwart and Bremner, 1976). 
It has been shown that gaseous S 0 2 , H 2 S, N 0 2 and N H 3 can be used as 
sole sulphur and nitrogen sources for higher plants (Faller, 1972). 
7.4.4. Vegetation—soil as sinks and sources of air pollutants 
The importance of vegetation and soil as a sink for atmospheric pollutants 
has been pointed out by several authors (e.g., Hill, 1971; Smith et al., 1973; 
Munn and Phillips, 1975; Ghiorse and Alexander, 1976). These sinks are 
important not only for cleaning the atmosphere, but also because many of 
the pollutants can be used as nutrients by plants and micro-organisms (e.g. 
S0 2 
andNH 3). 
Sulphur dioxide enters plant leaves through the stomata and at low con­
centrations photosynthesis can be stimulated (Knabe, 1976). Higher con­
centrations, however, cause both acute and chronic injuries to the vegetation 
(op. cit.). From data presented by Hill (1971), it can be calculated that 
vegetation around S 0 2 -emitting smelters in Sudbury, Ontario, could remove 
ca. 1 kg S ha"
1 day"
1 over an area of 225 ha. 
Nitrogen dioxide ( N 0 2 ) is emitted during combustion and can reach high 
concentrations, as for example in the Californian South Coastal Basin. As 
N 0 2 is rapidly converted to nitrate in plant cells, it can be used as a nitrogen 
source and the uptake of N 0 2 by alfalfa in the Los Angeles area could aver­
age as much as 0.28 kg Ν ha"
 1 day"
1 (Hill, 1971). 
Not only vegetation but also soils absorb pollutants/nutrients (e.g. Smith 
et al., 1973; Ghiorse and Alexander, 1976) such as N H 3 , S 0 2 , N 0 2 . 
The vegetation could in some instances also act as a source of air pollu­
tants. Miller and McBride (1975) when reviewing the effects of air pollutants 
on forests, pointed out that photochemically reactive hydrocarbons are 
emitted by the worlds' forests in the order of 175 Χ 10
6 tons y "
1 , whereas 
man emits only 27 Χ 10
6 tons. 
It should be noted that the processing of phosphate rock for fertilizer 
production liberates particulate fluorides that can reduce crop yields (Rich, 
1975). 
7.5. M A N I P U L A T I O N S (Frissel) 
There are many opportunities for man to manipulate or control nutrient 
cycles in agricultural ecosystems as described in Chapter 3. Because the data 
presented here deal almost entirely with the three main elements used in 
fertilizers, discussion was centred on several aspects of the use of fertilizers. 
These included (a) where in the world should fertilizer best be applied? (b) 
are fertilizers more effective in crops or livestock systems? (c) how can fer­
tilizer efficiency be improved? and (d) which of the three major elements 
should receive most attention?

--- Page 306 ---

303 
7.5.1. Future application of fertilizers 
The discussion was limited to agro-technological possibilities; political 
aspects — for instance, how such a planning should be realized — were not 
discussed. 
It was considered that one key question is: where should we apply fertiliz­
ers in the future, in developed countries or in developing ones? The idea on 
which this question is based is that developing countries, with their agricul­
tural production at a lower point on the "diminishing returns curve" than 
developed countries, may obtain a higher fertilizer efficiency than the 
developed countries. 
The answer to the question also depends, without doubt, on the goal 
which is strived for; social, economical or environmental arguments will 
probably provide different answers. Apart from that, the question is not 
quite clear; does developing refer to a certain climate or land use, or to the 
amount of mechanization available, or to the economic structure which 
must be available to transport and sell the products? Therefore it is better 
to re-define and split the question: 
— Should fertilizers be used with equal intensities in different climatic 
regions? 
— Should fertilizers be used preferentially on the most productive areas 
within a region, or should they be spread out over the entire area? 
There is no doubt that climates with most solar radiation have the highest 
production potential; a high production potential does not, however, im­
plicitly mean that the fertilizer efficiency is high. The production which can 
be realized depends on quantity and type of fertilizers, on the soil cultiva­
tion, fertility status, water management, pest control, maintenance and 
know-how. In connection with this it should be realized — and the point 
cannot be stressed enough — that "diminishing returns curves" are not 
fixed curves, but greatly depend on the aforementioned aspects. Water and 
trace elements may act as production-limiting factors, limited know-how 
may reduce the efficiency of fertilizer use, lack of pesticides may counter­
act the increase of the yield, etc. (Storage, transport and market problems 
often also limit the production, but are outside the scope of this discussion.) 
Limiting the discussion to the efficiency of Ν, Ρ and Κ as a function of 
the climate, the participants of the symposium agreed that in all climates, 
Ν efficiencies of 50% can be obtained for normal farming practices. For the 
production of rice in developing countries, this efficiency is often obtained; 
in developing countries the efficiency is sometimes limited to only 15—20%, 
and factors other than nitrogen are then probably limiting crop production. 
Limited knowledge of how to apply nitrogen fertilizers is another important 
factor. Fig. 42 shows that for farm inputs up to 150 kg Ν ha"
 1 considerably 
higher efficiencies (around 80%) can be realized. Systems with farm inputs 
between 80 kg and 120 kg Ν ha"
1 are: Noy-Meir + Harpaz-5 and -6 (Israeli 
semi-desert farming), Husz-8 (Central Brazil), Husz-12 (tropical and sub-

--- Page 307 ---

304 
tropical areas, S. America), Newbould + Floate-9 (winter wheat, U.K.) and 
Thomas + Gilliam-1 (corn, northern Indiana). All these systems are charac­
terized by a high technological skill, and they cover many climatic types. 
The conclusion may probably be that nitrogen dressings up to 120 kg Ν 
ha"
1 for developing countries can safely be recommended, if, and only if, 
the other production-limiting factors are eliminated. 
Most soils which were never supplied with fertilizers are phosphate-defi­
cient; thus phosphate is the factor limiting crop production. Many soils fix 
phosphate, and so only a small fraction of the fertilizer becomes available for 
the crop. Soils in temperate climates usually fix less phosphate than soils in 
warmer climates. Besides, many soils in temperate climates have received 
large fertilizer dressings in the past, so that the top layers of these soils (with 
a low fixing capacity) are almost saturated with phosphate and thus provide 
good phosphate fertilizer efficiencies. The systems Henkens-1 and -2 (Dutch 
arable farm with crop rotation) demonstrate this effect. Other examples are 
Husz-12 (tropical and sub-tropical products, S. America) and Thomas + Gil-
liam-5 (cotton, California) which suggest that in the sub-tropical and tropical 
regions too, a 100% efficiency for phosphate can be obtained. 
A general conclusion may perhaps be that because phosphate is often 
production-limiting in developing countries, phosphate dressings are recom­
mended. Because of the high fixing capacity, the efficiency will be lower 
than in developed countries for a long period. 
On soils which have never received fertilizers, potassium is probably al­
most never limiting for crop production (compare Fig. 52 and its discussion). 
If production is increased by fertilization with Ν and P, the need for Κ also 
increases. In general the efficiency for Κ is rather good; however, K-fixation 
by clay minerals necessitates much higher dressings in particular cases, as 
can be expected from the farm output. The fixation depends on the type of 
soil minerals. Apart from the availability of the elements, the type of soil 
minerals formed depends almost exclusively on the climate. Due to the for­
mation of the potassium-fixing clay minerals illite and vermiculite in tempo-
rate climates, efficiencies in these climates are relatively low for clay soils; 
sandy soils show no potassium fixation. The picture for the tropics is almost 
identical, no fixation on sandy soils, considerable fixation on laterite soils. 
For nitrogen there is yet another way of comparing the efficiency of fer­
tilization in developed and developing countries. For this comparison a dis­
tinction first has to be made between arable areas and livestock areas. The 
reasoning is that on arable land the efficiency of nitrogen fertilizers is as a 
rule often around 50% and, provided appropriate techniques are used, is in­
dependent of the amount supplied. Thus the conclusion must be that it is 
immaterial whether fertilizer is applied in developing or in developed coun­
tries. However, for livestock areas the situation is different. This is because, 
if limited amounts of nutrients are available, as in the developing countries, 
they are applied almost exclusively to arable land, while in some developed 
countries, especially in north-western Europe, grassland is also treated with

--- Page 308 ---

305 
a fertilizer dressing. Thus one might conclude that a comparison has to be 
made between the nitrogen efficiency on arable land in the developing 
countries and the nitrogen efficiency on grassland in developed countries. 
The efficiency with which grass takes up nitrogen fertilizers is about 60%; 
the efficiency of the nitrogen transfer between grass and animal products is, 
however, low, so that the overall efficiency between fertilizer and animal 
products is only 15%. (For actual values see Fig. 43; for farm inputs ranging 
from 100 kg Ν to 780 kg Ν per ha all outputs are between 5 and 22% of the 
farm input). Because there is no appreciable build-up of soil organic matter 
(see also section 7.3), 85% of the nitrogen must have been lost by leaching, 
denitrification or volatilization of ammonia. Therefore, in terms of feed pro­
duction a much better efficiency can be expected for fertilizers applied in 
developing countries than on grassland in developed ones. 
We have now touched, however, on a problem of much wider scope. 
Should we, in order to reduce pollution, or to feed more people, reduce the 
consumption of animal products? A discussion of this problem is, however, 
outside the scope of this paper. At the moment it is not even relevant, for 
it is possible to produce enough food for the entire world provided agri­
culture gets a chance to apply both industrial and biological nitrogen fixa­
tion to the best of its knowledge. 
The second question — should fertilizers be used preferentially on the 
most productive areas within a region, or should they be spread out over the 
entire area? — is much easier to answer. The fertilzer efficiency is highest on 
the most productive soils, and so we should give preference to fertilizer 
application on such soils and also try to remove the fertility defects. In this 
way the area required for crop production is reduced, together with labour 
and mechanization costs and the use of pesticides and herbicides. The area 
no longer required for food production can be used for recreation, roads, or 
for any other purpose. Unfortunately, social and economic arguments pro­
hibit such a development. Each country or group of countries is forced to 
be self-supporting to a certain extent. Within countries with a controlled 
economy this policy is, however, usually fully accepted, e.g. at the Interna­
tional Fertilizer Congress in Moscow (Artjuschin, 1976) it was announced 
that the U.S.S.R. will give priority to the fertilization of productive soils. 
A country like Israel distributes its water in the same way, available water 
goes to the most productive soils first; only when these soils are irrigated, 
are other soils also irrigated. 
7.5.2. Relative importance of 'N, Ρ and Κ 
Most participants were relucant to answer the question which of the ele­
ments Ν, Ρ or Κ is the most important one, as far as crop production is 
concerned. They are all of equal importance, all three can be crop produc­
tion limiting and they share this property with Ca, the trace elements and 
water.

--- Page 309 ---

306 
More interesting is the question which of the three elements will run out 
first in the future. Potassium provides no problem in this respect, leaving the 
choice between nitrogen and phosphate. The atmosphere contains vast 
amounts of nitrogen; industrial fixation is only a question of energy and/or 
price of the energy. Bioligical fixation occurs and can, without doubt, be im­
proved. A comparison of the production costs of the two ways of fixation 
did not provide a clear picture. 
The thermodynamic energy content of 1 kg N H 3 is 24 MJ. Chemical 
industry, due to all kind of losses, needs 78 MJ to produce one kg N H 3 
(Pimental et al., 1973). Biological fixation is still more expensive; estimates 
go to 480 MJ per kg Ν (Becking, 1971). The N-fixing micro-organisms, how­
ever, use photosynthetic products which may get lost if they are not used by 
these micro-organisms. Nitrogen fixation does not decrease the yield of the 
plant; as Rosswall pointed out, the micro-organisms cannot compete with 
the plant, the plant takes what it needs. 
On a radiation basis a plant—Rhizobium symbiosis uses 5.9 mg C in the 
nodule to fix 1 mg N, and 6.2 mg C to reduce nitrate-N in the root (Minchin 
and Pate, 1973). Therefore there is little to choose between sources at this 
stage in the cycle. Thus there should be little loss of yield in fixing nitrogen 
biologically. 
On a rather hypothetical cost of production basis 'biological nitrogen' 
costs 2—4 U.S.A. cents per kg N , 'chemical nitrogen' costs 14 U.S.A. cents 
per kg Ν (Hardy and Havelka, 1975). 
However, a farmer will only grow a legume if it is profitable, e.g. at pre­
sent U.S.A. prices of corn (yield 2500 kg per ha) at 10 cents per kg and soy­
beans (yield 750 kg per ha) at 20 cents per kg, the price of soybeans would 
need to be about 30 cents per kg for it to be profitable to grow it instead of 
corn. 
The availability of phosphate is also a question of its price. At the present 
price levels phosphate will run out first, but the earth's crust contains large 
Ρ sources which are mineable, from an economic point of view, if prices are 
increased further. 
A third viewpoint may be to which element priority should be given as far 
as research is concerned. The general idea is that the well known sequence 
Ν, Ρ, K, is applicable. This publication has shown that the nutrient balance 
of nitrogen has still many blank spaces. Protein deficiency is apparent in the 
developing world, and the possibilities of making biological fixation more 
feasible and economically practicable are challenging. For phosphates, the 
low efficiency is a subject which needs much attention. As far as pollution 
is concerned, the release of phosphates by run-off, and the leaching from 
heavily contaminated areas, e.g. sewage fields, feed lots (not particularly 
agro-ecosystems and therefore not listed here) may pose a problem. Phos­
phates in surface water are often the factor limiting eutrophication and 
should therefore be controlled as much as possible. For potassium, fixation 
by soil minerals is a particular problem.

--- Page 310 ---

307 
7.6. RESEARCH N E E D E D IN THE F U T U R E (Frissel) 
7.6.1. Missing data 
Transfers. As mentioned in section 6.1, all nutrient balances have blank 
spaces, and parts of the values listed are only indicative. Table 111 provides 
a rough indication of the needs for further investigations on nutrient fluxes. 
It is a summarizing table and, as such, an oversimplification. 
Pools. Some authors have supplied data for the nutrients stored in the dif­
ferent pools. Most authors did not, probably because the editor did not ask 
for it. There is no doubt that the amounts stored within the pools must be 
known to evaluate the importance of fluxes such as mineralization rate, etc. 
Different systems. For extensive livestock systems the main inputs are 
weathering, and input by rain and by nitrogen fixation, all three factors 
being not too accurately known. An advantage is that the consumable out­
put is low, the systems are therefore very near a steady state, and this facili­
tates the preparation of a nutrient balance. However, the outputs which are 
important for evaluating the impact of this type of system on the environ­
ment, such as leaching and release of N 2 Ο through denitrification, are almost 
unknown. 
The information available on extensive arable farming and extensive for­
estry is more complete, although for these systems too, N 2 Ο losses are un­
known. 
The mixed (self-sustaining) systems received considerable attention, and 
the fact that they are almost closed systems is a great help in collecting the 
data. Because of their importance for world food production and the abun­
dance of these systems, more information is required on: input by rain, 
(and therefore how nutrients are introduced into the atmosphere), weath­
ering, immobilization, mineralization, denitrification and losses of volatile 
ammonium. 
For all intensive systems the inputs are largely controlled by fertilizers 
and therefore well known. The uncertainties are especially leaching, volatil­
ization of ammonia and denitrification. 
Data collecting. There was a general concensus among the participants that 
good cooperation between specialists of several disciplines is required to ob­
tain reliable and complete nutrient balances. Part of the input and output 
data are within the direct field of interest of agriculturists, as for instance, 
yields and fertilizers. Other data, which are not soil-specific, as for instance, 
the input by rain, may be obtained from the literature. But data such as 
weathering require the help of a weathering specialist, who, in turn, can only 
supply help if he knows exactly the composition of the soil minerals, rain 
pattern and temperature. And even in this situation, as Schuylenborgh point­
ed out, a prediction is still difficult.

--- Page 312 ---

309 
Data banking. Husz explained the importance of data banking. Ecological 
scientists, trying to analyse agro-ecosystems need field parameters. Without 
such parameters, neither an ecologist, nor an agriculturist is able to give 
advice if he is asked for it. Rosswall, agreeing with the need for the data, 
rejected nevertheless the idea of data banking. It is dangerous to deliver data 
without any further information to people who cannot judge the worth of 
them. Most participants seemed not to favour the idea of a data bank, as far 
as typical ecological data (e.g. leaching vs. fertilization) are concerned. For 
well defined chemical or physical data (e.g. the cation exchange capacity, pH, 
rainfall and temperature), data banking may be very valuable. Newbould 
preferred cycling studies on long-term single ecosystems. In his opinion, the 
way to achieve progress is not by setting up a multitude of soil sampling and 
plant sampling stations. He proposed selecting 4 or 5 agro-ecosystems that 
cover the world situation, and examining them carefully by multidisciplinary 
approaches. Such investigations will open the way to more detailed model 
studies. 
7.6.2. More refined models 
An important part of the discussion was focused on the availability of 
nutrients added in the form of fertilizers or organic material. To the modern 
techniques applied to study this problem belong the use of radioactive or 
stable tracers and the mathematical modeling technique. 
Tracers have the advantage that a labelled compound (provided it is cor­
rectly labelled) can be measured very accurately. They also allow the investi­
gation of the way by which nutrients are lost. As discussed, nitrogen fertili­
zer efficiency studies show that about 50% of the fertilizer is taken up by 
the vegetation. Studies with
 1 5 Ν often show much lower efficiencies. The 
IAEA Technical report No. 121 (1970) which discusses nitrogen fertilizer 
uptake by maize, shows a wide range of efficiencies, the mean efficiency 
being around 30%.(The reduction of a report of 78 pages into one value is, 
of course, an oversimplification.) IAEA Technical report No. 108 (1970) 
on rice fertilization shows that the efficiency depends greatly on the type 
of fertilizer, mean values ranging between 15 and 40% (again oversimplified). 
Average values for wheat range from 20 to 40% (IAEA Technical report No. 
157, 1974). Such low values are not always reported. Westerman and Kurtz 
(1974) report efficiencies between 50 and 100% for Sudan grass. 
Nevertheless, it seems that in general the recovery from
 1 5 Ν experiments 
is considerably lower than could be expected from the balance studies des­
cribed. This can only be explained by a delayed availability of the fertilizer, 
i.e. part of the fertilizer is taken up in the organic matter of the soil and be­
comes available during the following years. Consequently the crop in a par­
ticular year uses part of the nitrogen which was supplied during the fore­
going years. This difference between the efficiency of nitrogen fertilizer 
applied only once, or applied annually is important and should be considered

--- Page 313 ---

310 
when setting up nutrient balance studies. The same reasoning is true for 
organic additions to a soil, but delays in nutrient availability for organic 
compounds are easily recognized. 
Simulation models 
The model described in Fig. 3 is, in fact, based on time intervals of one 
year, while many processes are actually dynamic processes. Mathematical 
models, so-called simulation models, are usually able to describe such dy­
namic processes with sufficiently small time intervals, for instance with 
intervals of days or hours. 
Fig. 53 shows a model in which the soil nitrogen pool is split into many 
more pools (Frissel et al., 1977). Nitrogen is present in the form of ammo­
nium ions, nitrite ions or nitrate ions. The main organic pools are biomass, 
humus, straw and manure. Transfers between the pools are described by 
microbiological or chemical processes. 
Interactions between different pools and flows are included by feedback 
control relations. The mineralization and immobilization in this model are 
described by rate functions; for each particular type of component (straw, 
humus, manure, beet tops) another function is used. The rates of minerali­
zation and immobilization depend on the time which has elapsed since the 
application of the organic matter and on the C/N quotient. Usually, Ν is 
taken up by the organic matter for C/N quotients above 20, and Ν is made 
available for C/N quotients below 20. The C/N quotient is calculated for 
I eaching 
Fig. 53. Simulation model to describe the behaviour of nitrogen in soil.

--- Page 314 ---

311 
zers) 
^ plants 
carbon 
t r a n s f e r 
volatilization 
atmospheric 
nitrogen 
leaching 
Fig. 54. Alternative split up of soil organic matter. 
each time interval again. Correction factors take care of the influence of tem­
perature, moisture content and 0 2 concentration. Lack of detailed data on 
such functions and the fact that for an 'unknown' organic compound first a 
decomposition rate function has to be determined, form a serious limitation 
for this type of model. 
An alternative model, shown in Fig. 54, overcomes part of these limita­
tions. All decomposable components which form the active organic pool 
(straw, manure, beet tops) except the biomass, are split into their compo­
nents which are proteins, sugars, cellulose and lignin. The core of this model 
is the growth of biomass using the separate components as substrates. If 
proteins are used as substrate there is an excess of nitrogen available because 
the substrate is not only used for growth but also as a source of energy. If 
the efficiency with which the micro-organisms use proteins is, for instance, 
40%, then 60% of the nitrogen of the proteins is made available. However, 
growth on the other substrates (lignin, sugar and cellulose) also depends on 
the nitrogen which stems from the protein source (or from other nitrogen 
sources such as fertilizers). If there is no nitrogen available the synthesis of 
biomass stops. Thus, whether nitrogen is made available or is taken up by the 
soil organic matter fraction, depends on the ratios between the different com­
ponents (protein, cellulose, sugars and lignin), the rates with which these com­
ponents are used for the synthesis of biomass, the efficiencies of these syn­
theses and the availability of other nitrogen sources. The reason that nitro­
gen stored in the biomass is considered separately, is because the biomass

--- Page 315 ---

312 
pool controls to a large extent the biological transfer reactions, and is thus 
needed in the calculations. The model described has already been tested by 
a single field experiment (Van Veen, 1977), but has certainly not yet reached 
a stage where it can be used for predictive purposes. Clay minerals are includ­
ed in the model because they fix ammonium ions; interaction between the 
presence of clay minerals and biological transfer rates, as proposed at the 
symposium by Filip could not yet be included in the model. 
Discussion of these types of model is certainly outside the scope of 
direct interest of a symposium like this one. Yet there must be consistency 
between data sets required for simulation models and nutrient balances as 
used in this publication. Therefore, close contacts between investigators 
working at different levels on nutrient models seems recommendable.

--- Page 316 ---

313 
Chapter 8 
SUMMARY (CONCLUSIONS) (Newbould, Frissel and Floate) 
The cycling of the plant nutrients Ν , Ρ and Κ in agro-ecosystems was in­
vestigated by means of nutrient balances. It was assumed that nutrients are 
stored in the pools: plants; animals; nutrients immediately available in the 
soil; non-immediately available nutrients in soil organic matter; and soil 
minerals. Thirty-one fluxes were used to describe the inputs, outputs and 
transfers within the systems. Agro-ecosystems were grouped according to 
their nutrient output (per ha) and type of management; the most characteris­
tic nutrient element is nitrogen; outputs are expressed in units of kg Ν con­
sumable product (consumable by man and/or animals). For extensive live­
stock and extensive arable systems the output varies between 0.4 and 20 kg 
Ν ha~
 1 . For mixed self-sustaining systems, outputs up to 113 kg Ν ha"
 1 
arable land were reported, for intensive systems the highest output mention­
ed is 400 kg Ν ha"
1 . 
From an ecological point of view the mixed self-sustaining systems are the 
most complicated ones. Because many of the important crops do not fix 
nitrogen biologically, such systems depend on a nitrogen catchment area. 
The catchment area often consists of pastures with legumes, enabling nitro­
gen to be fixed and transferred to the arable system via the manure produced 
by the livestock. The mean output of mixed self-sustaining systems depends 
mainly on the ratio pastures .arable land. For the paddy soil—homegarden 
system (south-east Asia) the irrigated paddy soil serves as the nitrogen 
cathment area; nitrogen is fixed by azollas and blue-green algae. All 
systems which use more than 20 kg fertilizer per ha annually are classified 
as intensive systems. 
Agro-ecosystems were described from: the United Kingdom, The Nether­
lands, Germany, France, Czechoslovakia, Israel, Japan, Australia, the U.S.A. 
and South America. Some of the nutrient balances are rather synthetic, 
others are derived from measurements carried out on particular farms. A 
description of how data for the nutrient balances were obtained is included. 
Results are summarized in graphs and tables. 
Warning: Consideration of the summarizing graphs and tables only, may 
result in generalizations which are misleading. Verify conclusions by con­
sulting the original nutrient balances. 
Many questions of importance to world agriculture and to the mainte­
nance of the quality of environment and landscape were considered in the 
discussions (Chapter 7) before the summarizing data as presented in sections 
7.1 and 7.2 were available. The editor has re-examined views expressed in

--- Page 317 ---

314 
the discussions in the list of the summarizing data. It is concluded that no 
new information has emerged to alter entrenched views on the relative im­
portance of future work on Ν , Ρ and K; of sensitive systems where leaching 
losses and pollution might occur; and of the efficiency of fertilizer use. The 
suggestion from some systems that the phosphorus in fertilizer is used with 
100% or greater efficiency obviously reflects either a complete lack of data 
on weathering and mineralization or the use of imprecise data (compare sec­
tion 7.2.2.). Few new data on the role of organic matter, and particularly its 
mineralization to release nitrogen and phosphorus, were presented. Although 
most systems appear to be gaining rather than losing nutrients in the organic 
pool, it was not possible to resolve the very diverse views on the role and im­
portance of organic matter to long term agricultural and natural ecosystem 
stability. 
Views on the optimum use of fertilizers for arable or grass systems or in 
the developing as opposed to the developed world, were hardly supported by 
data presented here. Likewise, little new stimulus was given to new proce­
dures of schemes of fertilizer use to further enhance world food production. 
What then has the symposium achieved? 
(a) It enhanced understanding of nutrient cycles, how they function and at 
what rate, and how they are controlled. 
(b) It brought together a vast amount of data which have only been analysed 
superficially at present. It is likely that a much more detailed analysis than 
has been possible in the time available will merit considerable effort in the 
future. 
(c) The collation and discussion of the data in a unified scheme have clearly 
identified areas with little or very imprecise data and have indicated those 
areas urgently requiring further research to understand processes as well as 
to provide data (section 7.6.1.). 
(d) It showed that nutrient losses to the environment due to high farm in­
puts exist for all types of systems and are often proportional to the farm 
input. Because high farm inputs can almost only be realized by high fertilizer 
dressings, losses to the environment are often proportional to the fertilizer 
use. However, with the available data, it could not be proved that the ratios 
Ny leached: N, consumable output and N, volatilized as ammonia: N, con­
sumable output are proportional to the farm input, so that for a given 
quantity of food which has to be produced it is not proven that losses to the 
environment are related to the intensity by which nitrogen fertilizers are 
used (section 7.2.1.). 
(e) Despite the imperfection of the data, there is sufficient homogeneity in 
the balances obtained over a wide variety of ecosystems, particularly as they 
vary in intensity of use or sophistication, to indicate that future studies of 
this type or further amplification of the present data will be worthwhile. 
Such studies should permit more precise assessments of relative fertilizer 
efficiency, of possible polluting situations, of comparisons between different 
parts of the world, than has hitherto been possible.

--- Page 318 ---

315 
(f) The realization that balances of the type calculated here represent the 
status quo or a retrospective picture of events only. The true situation is 
dynamic and it is to be hoped that the construction of simulation models 
will proceed in harmony with the collection of data, for they offer a real 
chance of forecasting events. For this to be achieved it is necessary to have 
the missing data, to have findings from the research considered necessary 
(see section 7.6.2.), and to have knowledge of compartment (pool) sizes and 
transfer rates over the whole range of time scales, which was not possible at 
the meeting. Finally, the predictions of such models will need to be validated 
in practice, either on single ecosystems or catchment areas or on average 
regional or world situations. 
A C K N O W L E D G E M E N T S 
The authors wish to express their gratitude for the stimulating remarks 
made by the chairmen of the symposium, Dr. Ir. G.H. Bolt and Dr. Ir. A . 
van Diest and by Dr. Z. Filip and Dr. R.G. Woodmansee. 
The editor wishes to acknowledge especially the help he received from 
his colleague Ir. P. Poelstra: Ir. Poelstra took care of many duties the editor 
should have done thus allowing him time to prepare this manuscript.
