Annex 1 
NOTE ON THE EXPERIMENTAL NATURE AND ORGANIZATION OF 
THE SYMPOSIUM 
From its very beginning the symposium Cycling of Mineral Nutrients in 
Agricultural Ecosystems was intended to have an experimental character. 
The foundation of the symposium was laid down in discussions between 
the Royal Netherlands Land Development Society (Vereniging Koninklijke 
Nederlandsche Heide Maatschappij) and Elsevier Scientific Publishing Com­
pany. The Royal Netherlands Land Development Society had plans for a 
series of meetings of scientific research workers aiming to contribute to a 
better management of the physical environment, and had set finances apart 
to arrange for the first of such meetings. At the same time, Elsevier was 
looking for a good opportunity to experiment on the possible ways in which 
the communication of scientific information through international confer­
ences and conference proceedings might be improved. 
A detailed report on the organisation and evaluation of the meeting can be 
found elsewhere (Hendriks and Manten, 1976; Manten, 1978). However, a 
synopsis is presented below, as it is felt that this will help the reader to 
understand and interpret the scientific symposium report presented by this 
volume. 
The choice of the symposium theme was influenced by considerations of 
two different kinds: 
(a) methodological questions, relating to the efficiency of information trans­
fer through symposia and symposium proceedings, and dealing, amongst 
others, with: 
— the optimum balance between pre-circulated information, lectures, and 
discussions in groups and plenary sessions, 
— making the best possible use of the knowledge, experience and intellec­
tual creativity incorporated in an interdisciplinary group of symposium par­
ticipants, 
— alternative means of recording and publication of symposium results, 
which might increase both the value and the readability of the collected 
information, particularly for those readers who did not participate in the 
meeting; 
(b) scientific concern about such questions as: 
— the need for food for a rapidly growing world-population, 
— the necessity for optimal use of our resources and energy, 
— the need to limit the pressure on the physical environment to a minimum, 
— the necessity to pay more attention to the landscape and to the conserva­
tion of valuable nature areas. 
It was hoped that the meeting would shed more light on the possibilities

--- Page 350 ---

348 
of higher food production for human society, without wastage of resources 
and energy, and with an avoidance of the extra pressures on the environment 
caused by possible wastage. 
Soon after the subject had been selected, the International Association 
for Ecology (Intecol) expressed an interest in scientifically co-sponsoring 
the symposium and participating in the organizing committee. Great support 
was received from the Institute for Atomic Sciences in Agriculture (Associa­
tion Euratom-ITAL) in Wageningen, who also provided the editor of the pre-
conference and post-conference documents. 
Authors were invited from different geographical and linguistic regions of 
the world. They were requested to provide data in the format indicated by 
an outline scheme which had been pre-designed by the editor and selected 
authors were asked to contribute sections. The scheme was based on: 
(a) a classification of ecosystems according to climatic regions and products; 
(b) a model nutrient cycle for Ν , Ρ and Κ with the system divided into three 
components only (crop plants, animals, and soil). 
The editor integrated the data provided by the authors into one compre­
hensive typescript. He also attempted to make some generalizations and 
raised questions for discussion. All of this material was circulated to the 
authors for consideration prior to the symposium and they were invited to 
think of additions, corrections and questions which they wished to be dis­
cussed. 
At the meeting itself, as much time as possible was reserved for discus­
sions. The latter took place in small working groups, which prepared written 
reports that were multiplied and discussed in summary-type general sessions, 
which were recorded on tape. There was considerable debate with regard to 
the establishment of a common set of definitions, how the data were to be 
presented for final publication, and why there is considerable interest in the 
study of nutrient cycles. 
The original outline scheme was questioned and there was general agree­
ment to a proposal by Newbould and Floate that it should be essentially 
altered in that the model of the nutrient cycle was to be extended by sub­
dividing the soil compartment into three pools. 
Individual authors were asked to revise their data to fit the new format, 
but it was not possible to rationalize all the data during the symposium it­
self. This meant that the general issues and questions were discussed at the 
symposium in the absence of detailed information. 
Authors were subsequently requested to carry out certain assignments 
upon their return home, which unfortunately not everyone did, while some 
others did not react within the time limits that had been set. 
After the meeting, the editor prepared a revised draft of the integrated 
manuscript, taking into account re-submissions that had been received from 
authors and the reports of the symposium discussions. He decided that the 
classification of ecosystems was to be based on yield of mineral nutrient 
(especially nitrogen) and type of agriculture. Authors were asked to com­
ment on the whole arrangement. We are particularly indebted to Dr P. New-

--- Page 351 ---

349 
bould, Dr M.J.S. Floate and Dr Ir G.J. Vervelde for the work that they have 
put into the revised draft. 
The final result no doubt still reflects the fact that the symposium was an 
experiment. Evaluation studies indicate that the tested conference method 
is basically useful and valuable, but can still be improved in several respects. 
The organizers are indebted to the editor and authors for their sportsman­
ship in so positively collaborating in the experiment. It is hoped that despite 
shortcomings in the final scientific report, this will appear useful and that it 
will also encourage the preparation of follow-up work, 
A . A . Manten

Communication Studies, in preparation.
